ABSTRACT
The interplay between sounds and movements is not only critical for music
performance, but also for the acquisition of speech, and might underlie the
success of using music as a therapeutic tool in the facilitation of movements. This
dissertation is comprised of three functional magnetic resonance imaging studies
that aim to elucidate the neural basis underlying interactions between the auditory
and motor systems in the context of musical rhythm perception and production. Study 1 investigated the neural correlates that facilitate auditory-motor coupling
while subjects tapped along with an isochronous rhythm. Auditory input was
manipulated so that the metric saliency of the isochronous rhythm increased
across five parametric levels in order to modulate subjects' tapping behaviour. In
study 2 the temporal structure of a rhythm was parametrically manipulated to
investigate the effect of metric complexity during movement synchronization in
musicians and non-musicians. Study 3 probed the response of the motor system
during action-perception coupling and decoupling: subjects passively listened,
listened with anticipation to tap, and tapped along with musical rhythms. Collectively, results reveal the important contribution of the premotor cortex in
the transformation of auditory information into motor actions. The ventral sector
of the premotor cortex may be important in mapping a sound to a precise
movement, for example, in the association of a particular musical note with an
effector for its execution. In contrast, the dorsal sector of the premotor cortex is
engaged during more abstract auditory-premotor interactions such as the selection
of temporally organized movements based on hierarchical rules ofmetricality. Taken together, it is proposed that these basic and higher-order response
properties of the premotor cortex allow it to be an important node for sound-
movement interactions during complex behaviours such as music performance.
v

RESUME
L'interaction entre le son et le mouvement n'est pas seulement essentielle lors de
prestations musicales, mais aussi lors de 1' acquisition de la parole, et pourrait etre
ala base du succes de la musique lorsqu'elle est utilisee en tant qu'agent
therapeutique visant la facilitation du mouvement. Cette dissertation consiste en
trois etudes d'imagerie par resonance magnetique fonctionnelle visant a elucider
les fondements neuraux ala base de!'interaction entre le systeme auditif et le
systeme moteur dans le contexte de Ia perception et de la production de rythmes
musicaux. La premiere etude examina les correlats neuraux facilitant le couplage
auditif-moteur chez des sujets produisant des battements alors qu'ils etaient
guides par un rythme isochronique. L'information auditive fut manipulee pour
que Ia proeminence metrique du rythme isochronique augmente a travers cinq
niveaux parametriques dans le but de moduler les battements produits par le sujet. Dans Ia deuxieme etude, la structure temporelle d'un rythme fut manipulee de
maniere parametrique pour examiner l'effet de la complexite metrique lors de la
synchronisation de mouvements chez des musiciens et des non-musiciens. La
troisieme etude sonda Ia reponse du systeme moteur lors de couplages et
decouplages entre 1' action et la perception: les sujets ecouterent passivement,
ecouterent en anticipant d' avoir a faire un battement, et firent des battements en
synchronie avec des rythmes musicaux. Collectivement, les resultats revelent une
contribution importante de la part du cortex premoteur lors de Ia transformation
d'information auditive en actions motrices. Le secteur ventral du cortex premoteur
pourrait etre important pour etablir la correspondance entre un son et un
mouvement precis, par exemple, lors de!'association d'une note musicale avec un
effecteur pour son execution. En revanche, le secteur dorsal du cortex premoteur
est recrute lors d'interactions auditive-premotrices de nature plus abstraite telle la
selection de mouvements organises de maniere temporelle selon des lois
hierarchiques d'ordre metrique. Ensemble, ces resultats nous permettent de
suggerer que les proprietes de reponse, de base et d'ordre superieur, du cortex
premoteur lui permettent d'etre un centre important lors d'interactions entre le son
et le mouvement lors de comportements complexes telle la prestation musicale. VI

## CONTRIBUTIONS OF AUTHORS

I am the first author ofthe manuscripts presented in Chapters 2, 3 and 4. My role
in this work included: conception of the experiments, design ofthe behavioural
and fMRI protocols, acquisition, analysis and interpretation of data, and writing of
the manuscripts. My co-authors, Robert J. Zatorre and Virginia B. Penhune, were
my advisors for this dissertation. Both oversaw the supervision of this work and
their roles included: providing feedback for the experimental stimuli and designs,
aiding the interpretation of data, and providing feedback on the manuscripts. The review paper included in Appendix A is divided into three main parts. The
first author, Robert J. Zatorre, wrote the section on the auditory system and also
assembled the manuscript into one coherent paper. I am the second author and
wrote the section on auditory-motor interactions based on findings and their
interpretations from this dissertation. The third author, Virginia B. Penhune wrote
the section on the motor systems. Vll

IX

Chapter 1: Introduction
1.1 General Overview
Interactions between sensory and motor systems are crucial as they allow us to
navigate and engage with our environment and with others around us. We have a
relatively good understanding of how the visual and motor systems coordinate, for
example during the reaching and grasping of an object (Rizzolatti and Luppino,
2001). In contrast, interactions between the auditory and motor systems have been
less studied, though they are equally important as they are necessary for speech
and music performance, among other processes. Thus sounds have the power to
shape and affect our motor behaviours; the topic of this dissertation is to
investigate how the auditory and motor systems coordinate in humans. The three
experiments presented in this thesis use functional magnetic resonance imaging
( fMRI), and have as their goal to elucidate how sound information is translated
into a motor representation for action implementation in the context of musical
rhythm perception and synchronization. This chapter is divided into two main sections. I will first explain why musical
rhythm is an informative model system for investigating auditory-motor
interactions, and second provide a review of the behavioural literature on rhythm
processing and auditory-motor coupling. The main purpose of this section is to
provide the reader with sufficient background to understand the rationale for the
selection of stimuli implemented in the experiments. I will then address previous
brain lesion and neuroimaging work on rhythm processing and auditory-motor
coupling, as well as summarize anatomical findings of auditory-motor
connectivity. This second part aims to demonstrate what we currently know about
the neural basis for auditory-motor interactions and will lead into the outstanding
questions that this thesis aims to address.

1.2 Behaviour
1.2.1 Why study musical rhythm? There are many ways to define musical rhythm, one of which is the temporal
arrangement of sounds according to their relative durations in a sequence. In fact,
when we think of musical rhythm, it is most often described of in terms of sound. However, there also seems to be a close link between rhythm and movement and
it is this relationship that this thesis sets out to exploit. Plato had defined rhythm
as kineseos taxis, an order of movement. A remarkable finding that demonstrates
the close association of rhythm and movement is the preference that infants have
in listening to rhythms whose beat they were previously bounced to, as opposed to
rhythms with a novel beat structure (Phillips-Silver and Trainor, 2005). Similarly,
coupling between sounds and movements also occurs when we have the
spontaneous urge to tap to the beat upon hearing a piece of music. This behaviour
involves listening to a sequence of sounds and extracting from it periodic cues
that consequently enable one to make predictions about when the next salient
event or beat is to occur, and implement an appropriately timed action with it. This seemingly easy task, apparently unique to humans (Patel, 2006), can be
performed by even those without any musical training (Drake et al., 2000b; Large
et al., 2002; Snyder and Krumhansl, 2001). The coupling between rhythm and
movement has also been capitalized in the form of music therapy. Rhythmic
auditory cueing is an efficacious technique that facilitates coordinated and timely
actions in people with motor impairments that are for example, manifested by
Parkinson's disease and stroke (Mcintosh et al., 1997; Thaut et al., 2007). Interestingly, the visual system does not seem to have as privileged a link to the
motor system, at least in the context of music. Relative to auditory-motor
synchronization, people are more variable in synchronizing movements with a
visual cue (Chen et al., 2002; Kolers and Brewster, 1985; Repp and Penel, 2002)
and cannot detect nor tap to the beat of a rhythmically flashing visual stimulus
(Patel et al., 2005), thereby demonstrating that rhythmic movement has a closer
affinity with auditory than visual stimuli (Repp and Penel, 2004). Therefore, it is

precisely the intimate association of musical rhythm with movement that makes it
an ideal system for the investigation of auditory-motor interactions.
1.2.2 Features of musical rhythm
How can a musical rhythm motivate actions? It is through the different types of
accent structures that certain musical events are highlighted, emphasized, and thus
rendered more salient than others within a musical setting; these accents act as a
cue which may facilitate movement synchronization. Temporal accents arise from
the organization or sequencing of each musical event in time, and can be sub-
categorized into grouping and metric accents. Grouping accents result from the
serial parsing of a sequence according to duration or gaps between temporally
adjacent events (Lerdahl and Jackendoff, 1983). These accents demarcate
groupings such as those formed by musical motifs, themes, phrases, and etc. Grouping accents are thought to stem from figural coding and/or Gestalt
principles; events are grouped or chunked together according to their shared
properties such as temporal proximity (Bregman, 1999; Handel, 1998; Lerdahl
and Jackendoff, 1983). In contrast, metric accents arise from the relative timings between nonadjacent
events that create the percept of meter, an alternating pattern of strong and weak
beats that divide music into segments of equal duration or measures. Meter is thus
the periodic organization of musical events over time that can continue to be
inferred even when the acoustic input ceases to exist (Cooper and Meyer, 1960; Lerdahl and Jackendoff, 1983). Importantly, meter is hierarchically structured so
that it consists of two or more levels of periodicities that are integer multiples of
one another, and where the most perceptually salient beat level is called the tactus
(Drake et al., 2000a; Drake et al., 2000b; Parncutt, 1994). Most people will
spontaneously tap at this level and it usually occurs at a rate of approximately
600ms (Fraisse, 1982). Furthermore, this commonplace ability to decipher meter
may be related to one's cultural exposure since a very young age to Western
music (Hannon and Trehub, 2005), that for example, is characterized by a high

frequency of musical events that coincide with points of strong metric accenting
(Palmer and Krumhansl, 1990). This higher-order knowledge of temporal
regularity demarcated by metric accents permits the listener to predict and
anticipate or expect the occurrence of future events. The dynamic attending theory
of Jones (Drake et al., 2000a; Jones and Boltz, 1989) proposes that listener's
ability to form these expectations is related to internal attentional rhythms in the
form of neural oscillators that attend and entrain to the different temporal
regularities or levels of meter (Large, 2000; Large and Jones, 1999). The sense of rhythm results from an interaction between grouping and metric
accents, though there is debate whether they exert equal influence or if one has a
more dominant role (Benjamin, 1984; Cooper and Meyer, 1960; Handel, 1998; Herbert and Cuddy, 2002; Lerdahl and Jackendoff, 1983). Povel and Essens have
demonstrated the close relationship of these accent structures in their model of
beat perception where the underlying principle is that temporal sequences are
assessed by a clock, internally generated by the listener, with a period that
matches the tactus of the sequence. Grouping accents that are distributed with
temporal regularity induce a clock, and result in the perception of meter that one
can then tap to (Essens and Povel, 1985; Povel and Essens, 1985). These grouping
or 'subjective' accents are derived as follows: for a temporal sequence where all
events are physically identical, isolated tones are perceived as accented, as well as
the second in a group of two tones, and the first and last in a group of three or
more tones (Figure 1.1 ). A hypothetical clock is compared to these accents and if
more 'ticks' correspond with an accented event, the stronger the clock or the more
metrically organized the sequence is. In another words, metrical rhythms are those
sequences that can be subdivided into equal intervals of time where these
divisions are demarcated by the coincidence of grouping and metric accents which
contribute to a strong clock or sense of meter. On the other hand, a weaker clock
results when the majority of 'ticks' coincide with unimportant events such as
unaccented tones or silences; these sequences are less metrically structure and
have been coined non-metrical. Metrical and non-metrical rhythms can also be

further characterized according to the temporal relationship between intervals in
the sequence, that is, whether they are integer (i.e. 1:2) or non-integer ratio (i.e.
1:2. 7) based (Essens, 1986).

Figure 1.1
Temporal structure of a rhythm: grouping and metric accents. This is an example
of a simple rhythm used in studies 2 and 3 (Chapters 2 and 3, respectively). Row
1 shows the musical notation of the rhythm. Row 2 shows its figurative
representation where events are denoted by a line and silences by a dot. Events
that are perceived as accented are represented with the symbol>. These are
defined according to the grouping accent rules by Povel and Essens (1985):
isolated events are perceived as accented, as well as the second in a group of two
events, and the first and last in a group of three or more events. Row 3 shows grid
marks of a hypothetical clock induced by a listener that is based on the perceived
accents. Each X in rows 4 and 5 represent a beat. Events with metric accents in
row 2 are highlighted by corresponding X's in row 4. An interaction between
grouping and metric accents occurs for example, at the eighth event where both
types of accents coincide. Lastly, the X's depicted in row 4 are related to those in
row 5 in a ratio of 3:1, thereby demonstrating the hierarchical nature of a rhythm's
metrical structure. The other manner in which musical events can be emphasized is via phenomenal
accents (Lerdahl and Jackendoff, 1983). These accents derive from the
manipulation of physical features of an acoustic stimulus such as sound intensity
and pitch, and are thus different from grouping and metric accents that are in
contrast, derived from the temporal organization of a stimulus. Nonetheless,
phenomenal and temporal accents can be closely related. For example, by playing

the first of every group of three beats louder, the metric accents of a triple or
waltz-time rhythm can be further highlighted. In contrast, playing the first of
every group of two or four beats louder further emphasizes the metric accents of a
duple or march-time sequence. In these examples, phenomenal accents serve as
additional cues to highlight the presence of metric and/or grouping accents. However, it is still not clear which specific features are the most salient for beat
entrainment. There is evidence for the importance of a joint -accent structure
where by a combination of accent types such as melodic, temporal and harmonic
interact to facilitate the perception of meter (Dawe et al., 1995; Hannon et al.,
2004; Jones, 1987; Pfordresher, 2003b). However most models of meter
perception have focused on the role of temporal cues (Large, 2000; Longuet-
Higgins and Lee, 1982; Povel and Essens, 1985; Todd et al., 2002) and studies
have shown their dominance over melodic cues (Drake and Palmer, 1993; Hannon
et al., 2004; Pfordresher, 2003b; Snyder and Krumhansl, 2001).
1.2.3 Role of meter in movement
There is evidence that listening to rhythmic music facilitates movements that are
coordinated, structured and purposeful, and that both phenomenal and temporal
accents contribute to this. Phenomenal accents can highlight temporal accents,
and both consequently allow us to create expectations of temporal regularity at the
tactallevel of meter perception. Rhythms with sound intensity accents on
metrically strong beats are reproduced correctly more frequently than those
rhythms without accents (Drake, 1993). Sound intensity accents can also act as a
strong cue in reducing the variability of subjects' temporal representation of a
rhythm (Smith, 1983) as well as orienting subjects, regardless of musical training,
to tap at the same tactallevel of meter representation (Drake et al., 2000b ). Similarly, metrical rhythms are better perceived and performed compared to non-
metrical rhythms, even in those without musical training (Drake and Gerard,
1989; Essens and Povel, 1985; Grahn and Brett, 2007; Summer et al., 1986). Furthermore, metrical rhythms where intervals are related to one another in
integer ratios are better reproduced than those with non-integer relationships

(Essens, 1986), however these differences are absent when rhythms are non-
metrical in context (Essens and Povel, 1985). It is proposed that metrical rhythms provide a temporally regular and predictable
framework that facilitates the ability to encode and organize large amounts of
information represented in memory (Drake, 1993; Keller, 1999; Palmer and
Pfordresher, 2003; Smith, 1983). In fact, accuracy of performance in rhythm
reproduction does not depend on the number of elements in the sequence, but
rather the number of underlying beats (Drake and Gerard, 1989). The hierarchical
organization of meter allows us to "link past, present and future events together"
(Pfordresher and Palmer, 2006) because it enables an appreciation of the temporal
relationship between adjacent and nonadjacent events (Palmer and Pfordresher,
2003 ), and thus may allow one to focus on organizing and coordinating actions
with other events nested within the framework. In contrast, non-metrical or
irregular rhythms such as those formed of non-integer ratios, by definition do not
have a strong periodic pulse and as such cause frequent violations of our temporal
expectations concerning when events are to occur. This reduces temporal acuity
since listeners must then implement alternate lower-level strategies such as figural
grouping, grouping by temporal proximity and counting to establish relationships
between adjacent events (Jones and Boltz, 1989; Jones and Yee, 1997; Keller,
1999). Explicit knowledge of metrical structure as in trained musicians, also improves
reproduction ofboth metrical (Drake, 1993; Franek et al., 1991; Kincaid et al.,
2002; Smith, 1983) and non-metrical rhythms (Watanabe et al., 2007). Non-
musicians are also able to use this higher-order strategy to help organize their
production of rhythmic sequences, although they still perform worse than
musicians, showing for example, distorted production of intervals within and
between groups of sounds (Smith, 1983). This suggests that despite a strong
metrical framework, lower-level strategies are likely implemented to achieve the
task in non-musicians. In particular if feedback is present, as during an auditory-

motor synchronization task, then this may also be used to monitor movement
timings (Pfordresher, 2003a). Interestingly, evidence suggests that musicians also
can code a rhythm via low-level strategies in addition to their use of metric
organization (Smith, 1983). In sum the perception and execution of rhythmic
movements involves both low-and high-level strategies that may be related to the
notion that rhythmic organization involves an interaction between grouping and
metric accent structures.
1.2.4 Direct auditory-motor coupling
Thus far, this discussion has shown how certain features of musical rhythm
influence motor behaviours. However, coupling between the auditory and motor
systems can also be of a more direct nature. Playing music involves the mapping
of auditory information in a one-to-one fashion to the motor system: a specific
pitch is linked with a specific effector that executes it. Studies have investigated
the strength and specificity of these types of direct coupling between the auditory
and motor systems. When arbitrary stimulus-response mappings are congruent
such that a key press matches an expected pitch, then timing accuracy as
measured by subject's inter-onset-interval (IOI) is closer to the target IOI (Keller
and Koch, 2006) and reaction times are faster (Keller and Koch, 2007) than
incongruent mappings. Similarly, for musically relevant stimuli, execution of a
musical chord or a two-tone sequence that is incongruent with its associated
sound results in slower response times compared to conditions where auditory-
motor coupling is congruent. However, this effect was only significant for
pianists, as non-musicians show no differential response between congruent and
incongruent mappings (Drost et al., 2005a, b). Furthermore, the disruptive effects
demonstrated with pianists are specific to their own instrument category (piano
and organ tones) as opposed to other timbres such as those produced by a guitar or
flute, and vice versa for guitarists (Drost et al., 2007). These findings solidify the
notion that sounds can be directly linked to specific movements and a perturbation
of this coupling interferes with action implementation.

1.2.5 Feedforward and feedback control
Music performance is a prime and sophisticated example that demonstrates the
necessary feedforward and feedback interplay between auditory and motor
systems (Zatorre et al., 2007). Direct sound-movement mappings, and the
facilitation of movements by auditory cues in the context of musical rhythm, are
examples whereby sensory input influences motor output in a feedforward
manner. Moreover, an internal auditory representation in the form of imagery can
also guide movements and thus influence motor production (Repp, 1999). For
example, well-learned sequences or performances do not rely on auditory
feedback (Finney and Palmer, 2003; Highben and Palmer, 2004). These findings
were first presciently observed by Lashley (Lashley, 1951) who noted that a series
of movements, executed for example during music performance, occurs too
rapidly for each act to be dependent on sensory control, and thus proposed that
movement sequences may be executed via a motor program. However, not only is a musical note or sound mapped onto a motor effector that
executes it, each sound must be performed in the correct pitch and in a timely
manner. Thus, each motor response generates a sound that becomes an index of
performance accuracy. Auditory feedback is important for online motor control as
it enables the execution of a sequence with temporal precision so that fluency can
be maintained between actions and sounds for music performance to be successful
(Pfordresher, 2006). Auditory feedback contributes to subtle expressive details of
performance (Repp, 1999) and is crucial during initial learning of a sequence
(Finney and Palmer, 2003; Highben and Palmer, 2004). Studies have investigated
the effects of delayed auditory feedback during simple finger tapping, and have
shown that if heard sounds do not match the expected sensory consequence of an
action, performance is disrupted. More specifically, period shifts where pitch
feedback is manipulated such that it is associated with an earlier key press,
disrupts the sequencing of movements by increasing error rates; in contrast, phase
shifts where the timing of the feedback is manipulated to occur after a delay,
disrupts the timings of movements by increasing their variability (Pfordresher,

2003a). Timing variability increases in proportion to increases in delay time
(Finney and Warren, 2002; Pfordresher and Benitez, 2007). Interestingly, the
disruption is reduced in pianists if the delayed feedback coincides with metrical
subdivisions (mentally counted out by subjects) of the produced inter-onset-
interval; however this effect was not specific as the reduced disruption occurred at
all probe delay times regardless of whether subjects were counting binary or
ternary subdivisions (Pfordresher, 2003a; Pfordresher and Palmer, 2002). Therefore, online feedback is important for the monitoring of our action outcomes
and knowledge of a rhythm's hierarchical organization as demonstrated by
musicians may also improve auditory-motor coupling (Pfordresher, 2003a; Pfordresher and Palmer, 2002).
1.2.6 Summary
The aim of this thesis was to devise behavioural paradigms using musical rhythm
that would allow us to elucidate the neural basis ofhow the auditory and motor
systems coordinate in a feedforward manner. The review of the behavioural
literature illustrates that phenomenal and temporal accents are salient cues that aid
the listener to extract the periodicities of a musical rhythm, and thereby facilitate
movement entrainment. Furthermore, musical training also affects the manner in
which these cues are processed, impacting on auditory-motor coupling. Therefore,
the experiments that comprise this thesis manipulate phenomenal (study 1) and
temporal (studies 2 and 3) accent structures of musical rhythms, and also assess
the effect of musical expertise (study 2). Importantly, we implement auditory-
motor synchronization paradigms where subjects are tapping concurrently with an
auditory cue so that we can simultaneously interrogate the brain regions that may
mediate interactions between the auditory and motor systems. The next section
will thus proceed to review the neural substrates engaged in rhythmic processing.

1.3 Brain
1.3.1 Lesion studies
Studies of patients with brain lesions can be informative in revealing cognitive
functions attributed to the lesioned tissue. Some have probed for deficits
associated with rhythm processing in those with various degrees of temporal lobe
infarctions that stem from stroke or temporal lobectomy; however, findings across
studies have not been convergent. For example, rhythm discrimination based on
same/different or regular/irregular judgements have been shown to involve the left
(Di Pietro et al., 2004) or right (Wilson et al., 2002) temporal lobes. In contrast,
others have found no task impairments resulting from lesions in either temporal
lobe (Liegeois-Chauvel et al., 1998; Milner, 1962). The perceptual ability to
discriminate between a waltz and march meter is unaffected after left temporal
lesions (DiPietro et al., 2004), but found to be impaired post right anterior
temporal lobectomy (Kester et al., 1991) or unilateral anterior temporal
lobectomies to either hemisphere (Liegeois-Chauvel et al., 1998). Complementary
to these findings is that the integrity of the right temporal lobe is also important
for tapping to the beat (Fries and Swihart, 1990; Wilson et al., 2002), a task that
requires the ability to discriminate meter. Lastly, reproducing simple auditory
rhythms from memory has been shown to be impaired by right (Penhune et al.,
1999; Wilson et al., 2002) and left temporal lobe lesions (DiPietro et al., 2004). In particular, the study by Penhune (Penhune et al., 1999) showed that
reproduction of visual rhythms was unaffected by temporal lobe resection, and
that patients were also able to perform the task using an alternate response mode
where the absolute timings of each rhythmic event was unimportant. In sum, these findings do not paint a coherent picture in identifying a specific
region within the temporal lobe or hemispheric specialization that is specific to
the different aspects of rhythm processing. These discrepancies may be due to the
variable type and extent oflesions across studies. However one interpretation that
can be derived from these findings is that the deficits shown in the motor
production tasks occur despite an intact motor system. This suggests that the

impairment occurs at the level of auditory processing and that without precise
timing or sequencing information being relayed to the motor system, the task
cannot be accomplished; coupling between the auditory and motor systems is
crucial.
1.3.2 Neuroimaging
With the advent of functional neuroimaging, techniques like fMRI and positron
emission tomography (PET) have made it possible to study and identify the neural
regions that mediate rhythm processing in healthy participants. Rao et al., (Rao et
al., 1997) were the first to use fMRI to investigate the neural substrates associated
with simple rhythmic tapping. The simplest type of rhythm is an isochronous
sequence where each auditory event occurs at a constant inter-onset-interval. They
found that tapping in synchrony with an isochronous auditory tone engaged the
sensorimotor cortex, cerebellum, and posterior superior temporal gyrus {STG). Continued tapping in the absence of auditory cueing additionally engaged the
supplementary motor area (SMA), inferior frontal gyrus, basal ganglia and
thalamus, findings that were interpreted to reflect auditory imagery, or the internal
cueing of the temporal interval. These core results have been more or less
replicated by other groups (Jancke et al., 2000a; Jantzen et al., 2004; Pollok et al.,
2005; Thaut, 2003), although some report the engagement of additional regions
during paced finger tapping such as the SMA, premotor cortex (PMC) and basal
ganglia, and others do not find significant differences in the neural systems
recruited for paced versus unpaced tapping. Most ofthese motor regions (SMA, PMC, cerebellum, basal ganglia) important
for isochronous finger tapping are also engaged during the reproduction of well-
learned metric (Bengtsson et al., 2005) or non-metric (Penhune et al., 1998)
rhythms, independent of the motor effector (mouth, right or left finger) used for
execution (Bengtsson et al., 2005). However, a direct comparison of these more
complex rhythms to a simple isochronous sequence might reveal the neural
regions involved in temporal processing when the number of movements is

controlled. Greater neural activity in the SMA, pre-supplementary motor area
(pre-SMA), cerebellum and posterior STG has been associated with the
reproduction of a metric compared to an isochronous rhythm (Bengtsson et al.,
2005). These regions along with the vPMC and pars opercularis were also found
to be more engaged for temporal as opposed to ordinal processing during rhythm
reproduction (Bengtsson et al., 2004), lending evidence for some specificity of
these regions in the temporal control of movements. Others have parametrically manipulated the complexity of a rhythm sequence in
an attempt to tease out brain regions important for rhythmic processing. Lewis et
al. (Lewis et al., 2004) used an auditory-motor synchronization paradigm where
participants tapped in synchrony with rhythms that increased in complexity as
defined by an increase in the number of different intervals comprising a sequence
(2, 4, 6). Furthermore, the intervals within each sequence occurred in mixed
combinations of integer and non-integer ratios (e.g. 1:2:2.5:4), thus, these
sequences are non-metric. Neural activity in the SMA, pre-SMA, dorsal premotor
cortex (dPMC), and dorsolateral prefrontal cortex (DLPFC) was shown to
increase as rhythmic complexity increased, regions that Lewis et al. suggest
mediate the selection of timing parameters and error-monitoring. The only other
study that attempted to manipulate rhythmic complexity in a parametric manner
asked participants to self-generate rhythms that increased in the number of
different inter-onset-intervals, from 1 to 5 (Dhamala et al., 2003). They found that
as rhythm complexity increased, neural activity in the cerebellum and thalamus
increased while neural activity in the basal ganglia decreased. Since each subject
generated different rhythms and movement rate was uncontrolled, these results are
difficult to interpret. Some studies have also attempted to determine the neural correlates of beat
perception and tapping to the beat. Sakai et al., (Sakai et al., 1999) were the first
to investigate the perception of metric versus non-metric rhythms, where the
former facilitate the perception of a regularly occurring pulse. In this study they

defined a metric sequence as having intervals related to one another in integer
ratios while those of the non-metric sequence were non-integer-ratio based. Perception of both types of rhythms relative to an isochronous sequence engaged
the PMC, intraparietal sulcus, STG and cerebellar regions. Specifically, they
proposed that recruitment of the prefrontal cortex and posterior lobe of the
cerebellum for non-metric rhythms is associated to the additional monitoring or
working memory processes required for the encoding of these more complex
temporal intervals. This interpretation is convergent with that of Lewis et al.
(Lewis et al., 2004) who used a synchronization paradigm described above. However, no direct statistical contrast was made between the processing of metric
versus non-metric sequences. Furthermore, while the stimuli were formulated
according to the Povel and Essens' model ofbeat perception, they did not take
into account factors such as grouping and metric accent structures that are integral
to the model, and thus some of the rhythms categorized as metric, were in fact
more non-metric in nature. A more recent and controlled study accounted for
these variables and showed that the basal ganglia and SMA are more engaged
during the perception and discrimination of metric rhythms in which accents are
regularly distributed compared to the perception of non-metric sequences (Grahn
and Brett, 2007). These regions were thus proposed to mediate the processing of
temporally structured and predictable, beat-based rhythms. In an ambitious PET study where the neural basis of dance was investigated, the
basal ganglia were also shown to be more engaged when participants danced to
the beat of tango music (metric) compared to its temporally altered version (non-
metric) (Brown et al., 2006), thus lending support for the role of this region in
processing beat-based rhythms. In contrast, the authors suggested that the anterior
cerebellar vermis was implicated in the actual entrainment of movements. To
identify this region, the study contrasted the main effect of tapping to the beat,
regardless of metricality, with self-paced movements. However, one might
question whether entrainment was even truly possible in the non-metric condition. Vuust et al., (Vuust et al., 2006) asked musicians to tap to the beat while listening

to the main meter or counter meter of a complex polyrhythmic song. They found
that the main effect of tapping to the beat compared to listening to the same song
engaged the sensorimotor cortex, pars opercularis, cerebellum and supramarginal
gyrus, and suggested that the pars opercularis is related to synchronization of
motor responses. At first blush, it may seem that findings from these two studies, along with others
previously reviewed have already identified the neural regions important for
entrainment of auditory stimuli with motor responses. To identify these regions,
these studies compared conditions where subjects tapped with an auditory cue to
self-paced tapping or listening. These analyses are insufficient to reveal brain
regions relevant for auditory-motor coupling as one can only conclude that for
example, tapping with an auditory cue engages certain motor areas compared to
tapping without a cue, and not brain regions that facilitate auditory-motor
coupling. Therefore the neural basis for auditory-motor entrainment remains to be
confirmed. Auditory regions including the posterior STG also have an important role in beat
perception. Neural activity that precedes an event can be detected by gamma band
activity (GBA) stemming from the auditory cortex and is shown to reflect two
processes. Evoked GBA is stimulus driven as it responds to physical tone onsets
in a metric rhythm and thus has been suggested to represent perception of the
actual pulse; induced GBA occurs not only at tone onsets, but also persists in their
absence which suggests that it represents rhythmic anticipations and expectations
(Snyder and Large, 2005; Zanto et al., 2006). When our rhythmic expectancies are
violated however, other event-related potentials such as the P300 are observed
(Brochard et al., 2003; Jongsma et al., 2004). Since the P300 occurs after the
event, it is proposed that it cannot be a direct correlate of rhythmic beat perception
{Zanto et al., 2006). These findings show that rhythmic sequencing whether in the context of
perception, production and/or synchronization, is a complex cognitive process
whose neural basis involves the auditory cortex along with several motor and
frontal brain regions. Before interpreting these findings, it is worth noting some
differences in experimental methodologies that could have led to the variability in
findings across these investigations. Studies that used fMRI and did not
implement a sparse sampling acquisition protocol (Bengtsson et al., 2004, 2005; Dhamala et al., 2003; Grahn and Brett, 2007; Jancke et al., 2000a; Jantzen et al.,
2004; Lewis et al., 2004; Rao et al., 1997; Sakai et al., 1999; Vuust et al., 2006),
commonly applied when auditory stimuli are presented, may have induced
possible interactions in cognitive processing of the rhythmic stimuli with the
rhythmical scanner noise. Also, different tapping rates such as that for the
isochronous sequences were implemented (intertap interval: range 300- 1250ms)
and the experimental context and data analyses performed varied across studies. Now, in an attempt to reconcile these findings, it has been suggested that different
neural systems are engaged depending on the type of time measurement involved
(Lewis and Miall, 2003). An 'automatic' system is recruited during continuous
measurement of predictable sub-second intervals demarcated by movements. This
neural system is purported to engage the aforementioned brain regions mediating
rhythmic finger tapping: sensorimotor cortex, SMA, PMC, thalamus, basal
ganglia, cerebellum and STG. In contrast, a 'cognitively controlled' system is
recruited when timing involves the discrete measurement of supra-second
intervals that does not necessarily involve movements (Lewis and Miall, 2003), or
according to a similar model, when more complex timings such as those in non-
metric rhythms are involved (Sakai et al., 2004). Characteristic of this system is
the recruitment of the prefrontal and parietal cortex mediating attention and
working memory processes. For example, the prefrontal cortex has been shown to
be more engaged as subjects become increasingly aware that the isochronous
rhythm they first tap along with gets progressively modulated in time as a
function of the inter-onset-interval (Stephan et al., 2002). However, it has been

suggested that once movements become skilled such as when a rhythm, metric or
non-metric, is well-learned and is part of a motor program, the automatic system
is engaged and there may be little or no differentiation between sequences with
varying temporal organization (Sakai et al., 2004). One can also interpret involvement of these multiple motor and prefrontal regions
in rhythmic tapping as being related to the fact that this seemingly simple task in
fact involves several sub-component operations. Details on the precise role of
these regions will be elaborated upon in the discussion, but in brief, some have
posited that the vPMC is involved in sequencing of rhythmic events (Janata and
Grafton, 2003; Schubotz and von Cramon, 2003) while the dPMC mediates
sensory guided movements (Wise et al., 1996). Others have shown evidence that
neurons in the medial motor regions (SMA and pre-SMA) code for specific time
intervals (Tanji, 2001), while the cerebellum may be involved in motor control for
the precise timing of events (Ivry and Spencer, 2004). Recent work has proposed
that recruitment of the basal ganglia in these timing studies are in fact related to
its role in the control of force during rhythmic tapping, a factor previous studies
did not control (Pope et al., 2005; Pope et al., 2006). Therefore, one outstanding
question is which of these motor regions might be pertinent for integrating sound
information with movements during rhythmic tapping.
1.3.3 Role of musical training
Many of us may have extensive experience listening and moving to music, but
formal long term musical training results in improved motor skills that are
associated with changes in brain anatomy and activity (reviewed in Munte et al.,
2002). In particular, it has been reported that musicians compared to non-
musicians, show a more efficient recruitment of neural resources indexed by less
neural activity in secondary motor brain regions (cerebellum, SMA, PMC) during
the execution of basic motor sequencing tasks. These tasks include: uni- and bi-
manual isochronous finger tapping (Jancke et al., 2000b ), finger-thumb sequential
opposition (Hund-Georgiadis and von Cramon, 1999; Krings et al., 2000), uni-

and bi-manual visuospatial-motor coordination (Koeneke et al., 2004), sequential
movements of thumb and fingers on a keyboard (Meister et al., 2005), and
bimanual sequential movements (Haslinger et al., 2004). In contrast, one study
used a serial reaction time task more complex than those described above, and
demonstrated that as learning improved, both musicians and non-musicians
showed evidence for motor neural efficiency, but musicians compared to non-
musicians showed greater neural activity in primary motor, prefrontal and parietal
cortex (Landau and D'Esposito, 2006). These results challenge the idea that neural
efficiency is a necessary marker of skilled performance and instead suggest that
reduced or enhanced neural recruitment is likely dependent on the specific task
demands. One important item to note is that none of these prior studies
investigated the temporal control involved in movement sequencing. Behavioural
studies reviewed in prior sections have shown that musicians are more temporally
precise at executing rhythmic sequences than non-musicians. Therefore, an
outstanding question is what the neural basis of this behavioural difference is.
1.3.4 Auditory-motor co-activity
The study of rhythm processing can offer insight into the neural substrates
underlying auditory-motor coupling, however, this topic can also be investigated
via action-perception processes in the context of direct mappings between sounds
and their associated movements during music performance. It has been shown that
if coupling between sounds and actions is closely linked, then brain regions
important for their integration are not only engaged during the perception of
music, but also during its production (Bangert and Altenmuller, 2003; Bangert et
al., 2006; Baumann et al., 2007; Hickok et al., 2003; Pa and Hickok, 2007). Neural regions identified in these studies that were sensitive to both auditory input
and motor output processes were the STG including the planum temporale (PT),
inferior parietal lobule (IPL) including the supramarginal gyrus, SMA, vPMC,
dPMC, and inferior frontal gyrus including Broca's area in the pars opercularis. Similarly, listening to music has been shown to elicit motor neural activity in
regions listed above (Lahav et al., 2007; Zatorre and Halpern, 2005), the insula

(Mutschler et al., 2007) and as well, the primary motor cortex of musicians
(D'Ausilio et al., 2006; Haueisen and Knosche, 2001). Importantly, these
auditory-motor interactions can be formed rather quickly within an experimental
session in those without musical training (Bangert et al., 2006; Baumann et al.,
2007; Lahav et al., 2007). However musicians compared to non-musicians, are
reported to elicit greater neural activity in the medial and lateral premotor regions
(SMA, preSMA, PMC), findings interpreted to be resultant oflong-term
associations built between the auditory and motor systems (Bangert et al., 2006; Baumann et al., 2007). Several motor regions have been presented as candidate areas for the mediation of
auditory-motor interactions, however one study has demonstrated the specificity
of this coupling and is thus worth noting in detail. Lahav et al., (Lahav et al.,
2007) trained non-musicians to play simple melodies on a keyboard. Subjects then
underwent fMRI scanning while listening to these same trained melodies along
with two untrained melodies; one that contained the same pitches as the trained
sequence and the other, novel pitches. Thus, regions important for auditory-motor
coupling, or action-representation of sounds, should be engaged in conditions
where the pitches were trained since the auditory input in these cases can be
linked with a precise motor output, the finger of execution. In contrast, this
coupling should be absent in the condition where novel pitches were heard. Brain
regions that showed specific auditory-motor coupling included the vPMC, pars
opercularis and pars triangularis, and to a lesser degree, the IPL and cerebellum. These findings were interpreted to relate to the mirror neuron system in terms of
an action-observation, hearing-doing network. Interestingly, it has been recently
suggested that congenital amusia may be thought of as a disorder in the auditory-
motor feedback system involving the posterior superior temporal sulcus (STS)
and pars orbitalis of the inferior frontal gyrus (Mandell et al., 2007). Together
these findings demonstrate that precise auditory-to-motor mappings involve
posterior auditory regions as well as the inferior frontal gyrus and vPMC. However, action-observation, auditory-motor coupling and imagery all involve

the linkage of action and perception systems and thus any one or a combination of
these processes may account for engagement of the various motor regions during
perception.
1.4 Anatomical connectivity
1.4.1 Connectivity between auditory and motor regions
Knowledge of how auditory and motor regions are anatomically connected is
crucial for understanding their functional interactions. Much of what is known
however is based on data from non-human primates. Direct connections have
been demonstrated from auditory regions in posterior STG and STS directly to
various:frontal regions including the dPMC, vPMC, BA 8, and BA 46 (Chavis
and Pandya, 1976; Luppino et al., 1993; Petrides and Pandya, 2006; Seltzer and
Pandya, 1989) via the arcuate fasiculus and superior longitudinal fasiculus
(Petrides and Pandya, 1988). There are also projections from these posterior
auditory areas to regions rostral and dorsal to the inferior limb of the arcuate
sulcus, corresponding to Brodmann areas 44 and 45 (Deacon, 1992; Pandya and
Y eterian, 1996; Petrides and Pandya, 2002). Subcortical connections include that
with the basal ganglia (Y eterian and Pandya, 1998) and projections to the
cerebellum via the corticopontocerebellar circuit (Huffinan and Henson, 1990; Schmahmann and Pandya, 1991). In contrast, the pre-SMA and SMA do not
directly connect with posterior STG, though they are connected to more anterior
regions of the STS (Luppino et al., 2001; Luppino et al., 1993). Lastly, the insula
could also mediate interactions between the auditory and motor systems since it
connects with posterior STG and the ventral premotor cortex (Mesulam and
Mufson, 1982, 1985; Mufson and Mesulam, 1982). (Figure 1.2)
1.4.2 Connectivity within motor and prefrontal regions
A brief review ofhow the various motor and prefrontal regions are connected can
also elucidate how these regions are engaged during a complex cognitive task
such as rhythmic tapping. Both dPMC and vPMC are highly interconnected
(Barbas and Pandya, 1987), with additional dense connections with primary motor

cortex and the SMA (Dum and Strick, 2005). In contrast, rostral sectors of the
dPMC and the pre-SMA do not connect with the primary motor cortex, but
instead receives projections from the prefrontal cortex (Picard and Strick, 2001). The vPMC however does receive influence from prefrontal regions including the
dorsolateral prefrontal cortex (DLPFC) (Barbas and Pandya, 1987; Dum and
Strick, 2005; Lu et al., 1994; Luppino et al., 2003), and shares connections with
neighbouring BA 44 (Pandya and Yeterian, 1996; Petrides and Pandya, 2004). The cerebellum sends efferent projections to the basal ganglia (Hoshi et al., 2005)
and both project to the SMA and pre-SMA (Akkal et al., 2007) and to prefrontal
cortex, in areas 9 and 46 (Middleton and Strick, 2000). The basal ganglia also
form connections with the vPMC (Middleton and Strick, 2000) and dPMC
(Tachibana et al., 2004). To my knowledge cerebellar connections with premotor
cortex has as of yet been studied. (Figure 1.2)

1.5 The experiments in this thesis
The aim of this thesis is to elucidate how auditory information is transformed into
a motor representation and thus understand the neural basis for auditory-motor
interactions. The review of the brain lesion and neuroimaging literature illustrates
that posterior regions of the auditory cortex and several secondary motor areas
including the medial and lateral premotor cortex, cerebellum and basal ganglia are
involved in the perception, production and synchronization to musical rhythms. Several of these motor regions are also anatomically connected with auditory
cortex. At present, it is unknown which of these regions are crucial for the
integration of auditory information to enable the execution of temporally
coordinated movements, nor is it known how neural processing of rhythmic
sequences differ between those with and without musical training. Furthermore
the presence of auditory-motor co-activity attests to the fact that these systems are
closely related, however, it is unclear how this relates to the mirror neuron system
of action-observation and imagery. Therefore, the experiments that comprise this
thesis attempt to elucidate the neural basis for auditory-motor interactions. Study
1 probes what brain regions might mediate the coupling. Study 2 asks whether the
motor system is sensitive to the fine temporal manipulations of a rhythmic
stimulus and how musical training might influence the neural processing of such
types of sequences. Study 3 attempts to dissociate involvement of the motor
system during the perception and production of musical rhythms. Chapter 2: Neural basis for auditory-motor interactions
2.1 Preface
The goal of study I was to investigate neural regions that facilitate or drive
auditory-motor interactions. This was achieved by implementing an experimental
design that progressively manipulates the degree of auditory-motor coupling. An
isochronous rhythm was manipulated across 5 levels by modulating the auditory
input parameters through the use of phenomenal accents. These accents, defined
in this study by sound intensity, progressively highlighted the metric structure of
the isochronous rhythm. We hypothesized that this change in metric saliency
signalled by the auditory input would result in concomitant changes in motor
output as subjects synchronized their tapping responses to these rhythms. These
changes would be evidence for auditory-motor coupling and thus allow us to
identify brain regions that coordinate sounds with movements. Chen, J. L., Zatorre, R. J. & Penhune, V. B., 2006. Interactions between auditory
and dorsal premotor cortex during synchronization to musical rhythms. Neurolmage 32, 1771-1781.
2.2 Abstract
When listening to music, we often spontaneously synchronize our body
movements to a rhythm's beat (e.g. tapping our feet). The goals ofthis study were
to determine how features of a rhythm such as metric structure, can facilitate
motor responses, and to elucidate the neural correlates of these auditory-motor
interactions using fMRI. Five variants of an isochronous rhythm were created by
increasing the contrast in sound amplitude between accented and unaccented
tones, progressively highlighting the rhythm's metric structure. Subjects tapped in
synchrony to these rhythms, and as metric saliency increased across the five
levels, louder tones evoked longer tap durations with concomitant increases in the
BOLD response at auditory and dorsal premotor cortices. The functional

connectivity between these regions was also modulated by the stimulus
manipulation. These results show that metric organization, as manipulated via
intensity accentuation, modulates motor behavior and neural responses in auditory
and dorsal premotor cortices. Auditory-motor interactions may take place at these
regions with the dorsal premotor cortex interfacing sensory cues with temporally
organized movement.
2.3 Introduction
Music can be a potent catalyst in stimulating timely movements. This
phenomenon is commonly observed when we tap our feet or nod our heads to the
beat of music. The ability to synchronize body movements to both familiar and
novel music often occurs spontaneously and intuitively, even for those with no
musical training (Drake et al., 2000b; Large et al., 2002; Snyder and Krumhansl,
2001). More specifically, the phenomenon of tapping to the beat appears to be
unique to auditory-motor interactions; visual cues are not as effective in
facilitating accurate movement (Patel et al., 2005; Repp and Penel, 2004). Furthermore, the important role of auditory cues in influencing timed behavior is
illustrated by studies involving subjects with movement disorders such as
Parkinson's disease and stroke. Walking ability, as indexed by the speed and co-
ordination of gait, and upper extremity arm function improve with training using
auditory cues, as compared to rehabilitation protocols without auditory facilitation
(Mcintosh et al., 1997; Thaut et al., 1997; Whitall et al., 2000). These data lend
support to the hypothesis that rhythmic auditory cues can influence motor
behavior; however, little is known about the basic rhythmic features and neural
mechanisms underlying this phenomenon. Thus, the purpose of this study is to
examine specific rhythmic features that drive auditory-motor interactions, and to
elucidate the neural basis of this behavior. When we tap, dance or march along with music, we are usually moving in time
with the beat. A beat is the basic unit of measure indicating musical time, and is a
perceived pulse inferred from a rhythm that occurs in equal temporal units

(Lerdahl and Jackendoff, 1983). The regular occurrence of alternating strong and
weak beats can be grouped together to form the percept of a meter (e.g. waltz or
march time) (Handel, 1989). Meter can help organize a rhythm by parsing it into
equal subdivisions or ''measures" of temporal duration (Palmer and Krumhansl,
1990). Within a meter, a hierarchy of multiple beat levels can also be perceived
(Drake et al., 2000b; Pamcutt, 1994) but the most perceptually salient level of
beat sensation, the tactus, is the level at which most people choose to tap to the
beat (Pamcutt, 1994 ). We propose that perception of the metric structure and
tactus facilitate movement synchronization to a rhythm and thus auditory-motor
interactions. Behavioral studies have suggested that there are several features of a rhythm that
can contribute to metric and beat saliency. Melodic cues such as pitch can
highlight important events in a rhythm, though not as effectively as a rhythm's
temporal structure (Hannon et al., 2004; Pfordresher, 2003b; Snyder and
Krumhansl, 2001 ). Ten1poral structure, defined by the pattern of time intervals
between notes, is an important feature that aids detection of the tactus by
organizing musical events into equal time units (Povel, 1984; Povel and Essens,
1985; Povel and Okkerman, 1981). The ability to perceive the tactus from a
musical rhythm is also aided by the fact that it is a regularly occurring cue. Such
predictability can create an expectation for the likelihood of future temporal
events even if the actual stimulus ceases (Cooper and Meyer, 1960; Large and
Jones, 1999; Pamcutt, 1994). Therefore the recurring pattern of temporal
expectations aids in solidifying the percept of meter and allows a listener to
accurately anticipate and tap to the beat. While pitch, temporal structure, and predictability are useful cues that highlight
the meter and tactus, in this study we are interested in how a rhythm's accent
structure can be an effective cue in facilitating movement synchronization. An
accent is a point of beat intensification highlighted by the manipulation of
physical properties of sound, such as intensity or duration (Lerdahl and

Jackendoff, 1983). Intensity accents can bring attention to a rhythm's meter by
emphasizing the relationship between individual beats (Drake, 1993). The
presence of intensity accents at the level of the tactus improves rhythm
reproduction. For example, Drake (Drake, 1993) asked children, adult musicians
and non-musicians to tap out simple and complex rhythms on a drum after hearing
them once. Rhythms that had accents on important beats (i.e. the tactus) were
reproduced correctly more often than those without accents, suggesting that
accentuation enables a more accurate perception and encoding of rhythms. Accents may represent a perceptual cue to emphasize important events which aid
the listener to organize other rhythmic elements into their appropriate places. The neural basis of entraining movement to a musical beat is little understood. Human lesion studies have shed some light on the neural correlates of auditory-
motor interactions involved in both perception and production of auditory
rhythms. These studies show that the temporal lobes play an important role in the
discrimination of meter (Kester et al., 1991; Liegeois-Chauvel et al., 1998),
tapping to the beat (Fries and Swihart, 1990; Wilson et al., 2002), and reproducing
simple auditory rhythms from memory (Di Pietro et al., 2004; Penhune et al.,
1999; Wilson et al., 2002). However, these studies have not converged on the
same findings regarding the specific localization and function of the left versus
right, and anterior versus posterior, temporal lobe. These discrepancies can in part
be attributed to the fact that the type and extent of lesions were variable across
studies, some encompassing cortical regions outside the temporal lobe. Several neuroimaging studies have examined the neural correlates of auditory
rhythm processing, including rhythm perception (Sakai et al., 1999),
discrimination (Platel et al., 1997), and reproduction (Lewis et al., 2004; Penhune
et al., 1998; Stephan et al., 2002). These studies, however, were not designed to
specifically address the auditory-motor interactions involved in movement
synchronization. Studies that ask subjects to synchronize finger tapping to a
simple auditory rhythm commonly show activation of the primary sensorimotor

area, ipsilateral cerebellum, premotor cortex, supplementary motor area, and
superior temporal gyrus (Jancke et al., 2000a; Mayville et al., 2002; Rao et al.,
1997; Thaut, 2003). The cerebellum has been implicated in tasks requiring motor
timing (Penhune et al., 1998; Spencer et al., 2003; Theoret et al., 2001). It has
been suggested that both the premotor and supplementary motor areas are
involved in sensorimotor integration, with the premotor cortex involved in the
selection and/or production of the response, and the supplementary motor area
involved in the organization of relevant sensory information (Kurata et al., 2000; Picard and Strick, 2001 ), such as the initiation of a sequence or sequence chunk
during motor action (Kennerley et al., 2004). Muller et al. (Muller et al., 2000)
has also suggested that the inferior somatosensory region could mediate a time
evaluation process involved in the synchronization of a tap with a click. However
recent studies have suggested that this region is more likely involved in tactile and
kinaesthetic feedback processing during movement execution rather than timing
feedback to the auditory cue (Pollok et al., 2003; Pollok et al., 2004). Thus far,
studies on rhythm processing have informed us that regions such as the
cerebellum, premotor cortex, and supplementary motor area are involved in
tapping in time to a salient auditory temporal pattern. However one cannot
distinguish the specific contributions of each region from one another nor can one
conclude that these regions facilitate, or drive the auditory-motor interactions. Two major goals of the present study are to answer the following questions: 1)
How does manipulation of a rhythm's accent structure influence the ability to
synchronize finger tapping movements? 2) What is the pattern of brain activity
associated with the integration of an auditory cue that facilitates an appropriate
motor response? We hypothesized that movement entrainment would be most
affected when a rhythm's meter is most salient, and the least affected when
listeners are unable to formulate a clear percept of meter. Furthermore, we
predicted that as subjects synchronized their tapping to rhythms with an
increasingly salient metric structure, brain regions driven by these auditory-motor
interactions would also be modulated. To address this hypothesis, five parametric

levels of metric saliency were created via progressively increasing the contrast in
sound intensity between accented and unaccented notes. Critically, since temporal
information (duration) about the stimulus is kept constant, our paradigm will
allow us to draw specific conclusions about how a higher-order cognitive process,
metric structure, can modulate motor responses.
2.4 Materials and Methods
Subjects
Twelve healthy right-handed volunteers (6 females) with normal hearing
participated in the study after giving informed written consent for a protocol
approved by the Montreal Neurological Institute Research Ethics Review Board. One subject was subsequently excluded as a result of incomplete data. Subjects
had no musical and/or voice training and ranged from 19 to 39 years of age (M =
27 years). Stimuli and Conditions
In this experiment, subjects imitated a series of six different auditory rhythms by
tapping in synchrony on a computer mouse key with the index finger of the right
hand. All rhythms were isochronous, that is made up of twelve complex tones
( 440 Hz fundamental with 9 harmonics, 50ms onset/offset ramp) of equal duration
(300ms) with a constant interstimulus interval (300ms). This resulted in an
intertap interval of 600ms which is in the range of the most common rate of
spontaneous tapping (Fraisse, 1982). Each trial contained the following events in
sequential order: 750 ms of silence, 500ms white noise warning sound, 1000ms of
silence, 7200ms of stimulus presentation (where subjects listened and tapped in
synchrony to all rhythms), and 90ms of silence (Fig. 2.1 ). The isochronous
rhythm was manipulated parametrically for five levels such that the metric
organization became increasingly salient. Intensity accents were placed on the
first of every group of three tones, resulting in a triple or waltz time meter. Five
levels were created, consisting ofO, 1, 2, 6, or 10 decibel (dB) attenuation
between unaccented and accented tones. Pilot testing was conducted to select

these levels; ranging from a level at which subjects could detect no difference
between the accented and unaccented tones, to a level where there was a very
noticeable difference. The sixth control condition contained the same number of
accented and unaccented tones (lOdB attenuation) as in the parametric rhythms,
but these accents were placed in an unpredictable or quasi-random manner. In this
way, no schema of meter could be created to aid entrainment and therefore
subjects would have no expectation of when the next beat would take place. All
stimuli were presented at a weighted average sound intensity of 85dB SPL, as
measured using a sound pressure meter in each left and right earphone, such that
the total sound energy was equivalent for all six rhythm conditions. Thus all tones
in the OdB condition were calibrated to and presented at 85dB and the range of
decibel levels for the other conditions was from 76.7 to 86.7 dB. These rhythms
were presented binaurally through Siemens MR-compatible pneumatic sound
transmission headphones using the software Media Control Functions (MCF
Digivox, Montreal, Canada) on a PC computer. Procedure
Prior to scanning, a baseline condition consisting of five trials of the OdB rhythm
condition was performed as a warm-up. Baseline data from each participant
helped establish the criterion for outlier or invalid responses for each individual's
test conditions. During scanning subjects completed two runs, each of which
included six test conditions and a silent baseline, for a total of seven conditions. Subjects were told that some tones would be louder/softer than others but that the
task was to focus on synchronizing their tap responses to the tones as accurately
as possible. With the exception of the silent baseline, each test condition was
presented in blocks of 12 trials, ensuring enough time for subjects to be fully
entrained to the stimuli. Two silent trials of the same duration as the rhythm trials
were interspersed between each test block. Test conditions were presented in a
pseudo-random order within each run across all subjects and all conditions were
performed with eyes closed. For all conditions, tap responses (key onset and
offset times) were collected online.

fMRI acquisition
Scanning was performed on a 1.5 T Siemens Sonata imager. High resolution T1-
weighted anatomical scans were collected for each subject (voxel size:
1x1x1mm3, matrix size: 256x256). A total of85 frames was obtained for each of
two runs in the functional T2*-weighted gradient echo planar scans (12 frames
per condition per run). Whole head interleaved scans (n=25) were taken, oriented
in a direction orthogonal to that of the Sylvian Fissure (TE=50ms, TR=11790ms,
voxel size: 5x5x5mm3, matrix size: 64x64x25, FOV: 320mm2) (Fig. 2.1). A
single-trial sparse-sampling design was used whereby scan acquisition occurred
after each trial presentation, when the subject ceased to perform the task. Since
the hemodynamic response is delayed, scan acquisition would thus coincide with
neural activity associated with rhythm entrainment and not with the initial portion
of the task when the entrainment process has yet to commence. Moreover, a long
TR was used so that subjects would be able to hear the stimuli without the loud,
rhythmical scanner noise that could interfere with the task, a potential confound
present in some studies that have examined auditory rhythm processing using
fMRI (Jancke et al., 2000a; Lewis et al., 2004; Mayville et al., 2002; Rao et al.,
1997). Furthermore, a single trial design avoids contamination of the blood-
oxygenation level dependent (BOLD) response to the auditory stimuli with the
BOLD response to the acquisition noise (Belin et al., 1999; Hall et al., 1999).
fMRI analyses
The first volume of each functional run was discarded. Images from each scan
were then realigned with the third frame as reference, motion corrected using the
AFNI software (Cox, 1996), and smoothed using a 12mm full-width half-
maximum (fwhm) isotropic Gaussian kernel. For each subject, the anatomical and
functional volumes were transformed into standard stereotaxic space (Talairach
and Tournoux, 1988) based on the MNI 305 template (Collins et al., 1994). Statistical analysis of fMRI data was based on a linear model performed using an
in-house tool called fMRIST AT that involves a set of four MATLAB functions
(Worsley et al., 2002) (available at www.math.mcgill.ca/keith/finristat). The

general linear model Y = XP + e is an equation that expresses the response
variable (BOLD signal) Y, in terms of a linear combination of the explanatory
variable (stimulus) X, the parameter estimates (effects of interest) p, and the error
term e. Temporal drift is modeled as cubic splines and then removed since it can
be confounded with the BOLD response. The first matlab function 'finridesign'
sets up the design matrix, where each column contains the explanatory variables
and each row represents a scan. The next step 'finrilm' fits the linear model with
the fMRI time series, solving the parameter estimates p, in the least squares sense
and yielding estimates of effects, standard errors, and t-statistics for each contrast,
for each run. An effect of interest is specified by a vector of contrast weights that
give a weighted sum or compound of parameter estimates referred to as a contrast. To determine the basic neural network associated with simple finger tapping to an
unaccented auditory cue, we first contrasted the OdB - silence conditions. To
directly address changes in neural activity related to the five levels of
accentuation, we performed a covariation analysis. This analysis examines the
relationship between BOLD response and increasing accentuation across the five
parametric conditions (0, 1, 2, 6, 10dB), where each condition is assigned a value
from 1 to 5, assuming a linear response in this variable. In this case the parameter
estimates represent the covariation of the BOLD response with the linear
measures 1 to 5. The!-statistical map obtained for this analysis thus assesses
whether the slope of the regression line at each voxel is significantly different
from zero. The third MATLAB program 'multistat' combines runs together within
subjects (using a fixed-effects model), and then results from each subject are
combined to generate group statistical maps for each contrast of interest. A mixed
effects model is used in averaging data across subjects; the data are smoothed
with a fwhm Gaussian filter so that the ratio of the random-effects variance
divided by the fixed-effects variance results in 100 degrees of freedom. Lastly, the
program 'stat_summary' assesses the threshold for significance using the
minimum given by a Bonferroni correction, random field theory, and the discrete
local maximum to account for multiple comparisons (yY orsley, 2005). The
threshold for a significant peak wast= 4.47 at p<0.05, using a whole brain search

volume. Peaks below this threshold, but contralateral to significant regions are
also reported since they have a high likelihood of representing real effects as
opposed to false positives. Functional connectivity allows us to determine how neural activity at a pre-chosen
reference (or seed) voxel correlates with all other voxels in the brain across time. To determine how functional connectivity is modulated by the parametric
stimulus manipulation, a variant of the psychophysiological interactions method
proposed by Friston et al. (Friston et al., 1997) (see
www.math.mcgill.ca/keith/fmristat) was performed for regions identified from the
covariation analysis. In modeling the stimulus modulated changes in temporal
coherence, the effects of the stimulus are accounted for such that correlations are
between the voxels of interest and not with those of the stimulus already identified
from the covariation analysis. Thus in the general linear model, an interaction
product between the stimulus (X) and reference voxel value (R) is added as a
regressor variable at each time point for every voxel and is solved for: Yij = Xi~lj
+ Ri~2j + XiRi~3j +£,where Yij is the voxel value at each frame i, for each voxel j. Slice timing correction is also implemented and the voxel values at each frame are
extracted from native space. The effect, standard error, and t-statistic are then
estimated using fMRISTAT as described previously. Positive t-statistics show
voxels whose temporal correlation with the reference voxel is increased as a
function of stimulus salience and vice versa for the negative t-statistics. Since we
had an a priori hypothesis concerning the regions that would demonstrate
stimulus-modulated functional connectivity, other regions that were also
identified from this analysis are not reported, and the significance threshold of
t=2.64 at p<0.005 uncorrected was used.

2.5 Results
Behavioral Data
To assess subjects' response to the different levels of rhythmic accentuation, the
dependent variable used was the tap duration, the reproduction of the interval
between tone onset to tone offset. Tap duration was calculated for each tap
subjects made, and averaged across the accented and unaccented elements for
each condition. Individual subjects' data were filtered to exclude outliers or
invalid responses based on each person's baseline performance data, following the
procedure described by Penhune et al. (Penhune et al., 1998). Figure 2.2 shows
six graphs representing the six conditions, and in each graph, the tap durations
corresponding to each tone of the rhythm, averaged across all subjects. Inspection
of these raw data showed that subjects appear to entrain to the rhythms from tone
4 onwards. Thus behavioral performance was analyzed using the valid responses
excluding the first three tap responses. The percentage of valid responses did not
differ (approx. 98%) across conditions (One-way repeated measures ANOVA, F(5, 50) = 0.83, p>0.5). Tap duration was used to assess the relationship of the
averaged accented and unaccented tones across the parametric conditions. A
significant linear correlation was demonstrated, such that as saliency of the metric

structure increased across the five parametric conditions, tap durations of accented
tones also increased (r=0.99, t=12.12, p<0.005); this trend was not present for the
tap durations ofunaccented tones (r= -0.73, t=-1.84, p=0.16) (Fig. 2.3). Planned
comparisons verified that the accented and unaccented tones in the 1 OdB
condition differ from each other (F(l,l0)=14.91, p<0.005) where as those in the
random condition did not (F{l,10)=0.70, p=0.42). To summarize, reproduction of the tap duration was relatively lengthened for
those tones that were accented. This effect of intensity accentuation became more
pronounced as the intensity cues signaling the metric structure became more
salient across the 0- lOdB conditions. The linearity ofthe response validated our
choice of dB differences as corresponding roughly to perceptually equal
increments. In addition, metric structure appeared to influence cue saliency
because accented elements in the random condition were not lengthened
compared to the unaccented elements.

fMRI Data
Subtraction Analysis
A subtraction of the OdB versus the silent condition showed brain regions
involved in the synchronization of a simple tapping response to isochronous
rhythms without effects of accentuation. These regions are bilateral posterior
superior temporal gyrus (STG), left primary motor cortex, left thalamus, and right
cerebellum lobule V (as determined from the atlas of Schmahmann et al.
(Schmahmann et al., 2000)) (Table 2.1)., Region of Peak

The results of the behavioral data showed a significant linear correlation between
increasing metric saliency and tap duration; thus to reveal brain regions that
co varied as a function of metric saliency we performed a parametric analysis with
5 levels, corresponding to OdB, ldB, 2dB, 6dB, and 10dB. The only regions found
to covary with metric saliency were the left planum temporale, as determined by
anatomical probability maps (Westbury et al., 1999), the right posterior STG, and

Since the covariation analysis established that both posterior STG and dPMC
covaried with the parametric stimulus manipulation, a functional connectivity
analysis was performed to assess how temporal correlations between these two
regions might be modulated by the stimulus. The voxels of the left and right
dPMC identified from the covariation analysis were each taken as reference seed
voxels to look for stimulus-modulated correlations with any of the pre-identified
posterior STG voxels. An interaction between the stimulus and connectivity in the
left dPMC was found in bilateral dPMC and left Heschl's gyrus as determined by
anatomical probability maps (Penhune et al., 1996) (Table 2.3; Fig. 2.5). Similarly, stimulus modulated connectivity in the right dPMC was found to occur
