the% BOLD signal change in left and right voxels in the posterior STG across the
different experimental conditions for each rhythm type, metric simple (MS),
metric complex (MC) and non-metric (NM). Neural activity in the left posterior
STG (left graph, black bars) correlates with rhythm complexity only in the passive
listen condition: as rhythm complexity increases, neural activity decreases. Decreased neural activity during listen with anticipation and tap may be related to
motor-induced suppression of auditory cortex.
5.2.2 Auditory processing streams
As just discussed in the prior section, processing of auditory information at the
cortical level begins in the PAC and likely proceeds to the PT. At the PT, auditory
spectrotemporal information is disambiguated and then gated to relevant higher-
order cortical regions for further analysis. From where would rhythmic sequences
proceed to be analyzed? One of the first models of auditory processing
(Rauschecker and Tian, 2000) paralleled that of vision (Mishkin et al., 1983) in
proposing that spatial information from the acoustic input is relayed to the parietal
cortex (i.e. via the dorsal stream), whereas object information is transmitted to
anterior temporal regions for object recognition (i.e. via the ventral stream). This
model characterizes the auditory processing streams as being sensitive to
properties of the stimulus, object versus space. If this were the case, findings from
this thesis should have demonstrated engagement of anterior temporal lobe
regions in mediating the recognition of a musical rhythm, especially since there
was no spatial component to the tasks utilized. However, this was not the case as
only the posterior aspect of the STG encompassing the PT was recruited. A subsequent model of auditory processing suggested that the dorsal auditory
stream could also be defined according to the type of computation needed to be
performed, rather than by properties of the stimulus (Zatorre and Belin, 2005). For
example in the visual domain, stimulation of the retina by the distribution of light
signals the position and movement of objects in space, thus leading to engagement
of the dorsal visual stream in spatial processing. However, stimulation of the
basilar membrane by sound does not signal spatial position, but sound frequency. Furthermore, the conception of an auditory object was also re-defined to represent
the source of a sound (e.g. piano) as opposed to the sound pattern conveyed by the

source (e.g. rhythm). Therefore in the model of Zatorre and Belin, the dorsal
auditory pathway is suggested to represent the distribution of spectral energy over
time, or sound patterns such as a musical rhythm. This would be consistent with
the notion that movements, especially those guided by an auditory cue such as
music, occurs over time and across space. Once sounds have been analyzed in auditory cortex, it is proposed that the dorsal
auditory stream then communicates with motor, premotor and prefrontal regions
such that actions can then be implemented based on information conveyed by the
acoustic input (Warren et al., 2005). This model parallels that in the visual domain
where the dorsal stream has also been described to mediate actions, and the
ventral stream, perceptual processing (Milner and Goodale, 1995). This
dissertation makes an important contribution to the field of sensorimotor research
as it addresses two important issues that stem from the model ofWarren et al. First, their proposal is based on evidence from speech, animal vocalizations and
auditory spatial processes, and the authors speculate whether "activation of the
pathway [can] be demonstrated for non-verbal and non-spatial motor outputs such
as non-verbal emotional vocalizations and musical performance". Furthermore,
while evidence was provided to show that sounds elicit motor neural activity, the
model did not focus on how these motor regions, such as the premotor cortex,
could support sensorimotor functions. Instead, it expanded upon the role of
auditory areas such as the posterior superior temporal plane. Therefore in the
proceeding section, I will provide a hypothesis for how the premotor cortex
intercepts sound information bearing motor instructions that are relevant for
music performance. These ideas have been presented in a review paper (Zatorre et
al., 2007) that is included in the appendix of this thesis (Appendix A).
5.2.3 Role of the premotor cortex in auditory-motor transformations
Several reasons lead to the proposal that the premotor cortex is an important node
for auditory-motor transformations. One important piece of evidence comes from
anatomy: the premotor cortex is the only cortical motor brain area that directly
Ill

connects with posterior STG and with the primary motor cortex (M1), thus
potentially enabling auditory-guided actions to be immediately implemented. In
contrast, other cortical regions that connect with posterior STG do not have direct
access to M1. The review of the functional neuroimaging literature in the
introduction showed that several motor regions were involved in rhythm
processing and auditory-motor coupling; however, these studies did not
demonstrate specificity of any motor region in auditory-motor transformations. Findings from this thesis address this issue in the context of music, and
corroborate what is already known about the premotor cortex in its role of
computing visuomotor transformations. Therefore, based on findings in the vision
domain I will argue in the following sections that the premotor cortex may have a
generic role in sensorimotor transformations, including those relevant in the
auditory domain for music perception and production. The premotor cortex is divided into dorsal (dPMC) and ventral (vPMC) sectors
that are approximately demarcated at a point inferior to the junction of the
superior frontal sulcus with the superior precentral sulcus, at a Talairach location
of z-50 (Rizzolatti and Craighero, 2004; Tomassini et al., 2007). Based on
considerable evidence from electrophysiological research, Hoshi and Tanji (Hoshi
and Tanji, 2006, 2007) propose that the vPMC and dPMC are involved in direct
and indirect visuomotor transformations, respectively. Direct transformations
involve a "one-to-one" matching of sensory features with motor acts (Fogassi et
al., 2001; Hoshi and Tanji, 2006; Kakei et al., 2001; Murata et al., 1997). In the
classic reach and grasp example, neurons in the vPMC represent sensory
properties of the target: they match properties of the visual object (e.g. shape of an
apple) with an appropriate motor gesture (e.g. shape ofhand). Inactivation of
these neurons causes impaired hand shaping such that objects cannot be grasped,
despite the absence of motor paralysis or a sensory deficit (Fogassi et al., 2001). Neurons in vPMC also transform targets from visual-into-motor frames of
reference such that a seen object can be reached from the appropriate direction
(Kakei et al., 2001). Direct auditory-motor transformations are highly relevant during music
performance. As reviewed in the introduction, playing music requires precise
auditory-motor mapping between the sound of a note and the effector used to
produce that note, and in particular, has been shown to engage the vPMC and BA
44 (Bangert et al., 2006; Baumann et al., 2007; Lahav et al., 2007). Findings from
study 3 also support this idea, as an area at the border ofvPMC and BA 44 was
recruited when sounds were meaningful to the motor system, that is, during the
listen with anticipation to tap, and tap conditions. The vPMC was not engaged
during the naive passive listen condition when sounds were of no relevance for
the motor system. This finding is consistent with those of Lahav et al. (Lahav et
al., 2007) who demonstrated engagement of the vPMC and BA 44 when subjects
listened to simple melodies that they had been trained to play, but not to novel
melodies that were motorically unfamiliar. Furthermore, neural activity in the
vPMC was no different across the three levels of rhythmic complexity, thus
demonstrating its insensitivity to the higher-order features embedded in an
auditory stimulus. Therefore, the vPMC is active upon hearing music for which
one has an associated motor program and is thus proposed to mediate the direct
transformation of sounds to movements. On the other hand, the dPMC is thought to have a more indirect role in
sensorimotor transformations: it represents motor information instructed by the
sensory cues rather than their sensory properties (Hoshi and Tanji, 2006; Kurata
and Hoffman, 1994; Wise et al., 1996). In the reach and grasp example, dPMC
neurons are involved in motor planning, preparing and selecting movement
parameters (e.g. direction and amplitude) in response to what the sensory cues
signal. They combine and convey information about where to reach, what hand to
reach with, and how to reach. Thus, neurons in dPMC retrieve and integrate
sensory information with motor instructions in order to carry out an action plan
(Hoshi and Tanji, 2006, 2007). The rostral dPMC is of particular interest because
it participates in more abstract or higher-order aspects of movement (Cisek and

Kalaska, 2004; Hoshi and Tanji, 2006; Petrides, 2005b; Picard and Strick, 2001),
such as the selection of movements that are conditionally linked by a sensory
stimulus (Halsband and Freund, 1990; Passingham, 1985; Petrides, 1985a; Schluter et al., 1998). In this case, the sensory signal does not directly indicate an
action per se, but rather a conditional rule about what movement to select among
competing alternatives, a function that would be highly useful for musical
execution, which depends on learned actions and a hierarchical organization. Inactivation of the dPMC, not vPMC, impairs these conditional motor behaviours
(Kurata and Hoffinan, 1994 ), and also the ability to coordinate and time
movements (Davare et al., 2006}, another critical feature for musical performance. The view that the dPMC is involved in higher-order aspects of movement
organization is supported by findings from studies 1 and 2 (with replication of
findings in study 3) where the abstract metrical structure of a musical rhythm was
manipulated during auditory-motor synchronization. It is important to note that I
do not suggest that the dPMC codes for how metrically structured a rhythm is. If
so, then results from studies 1 and 2 would be in conflict with each other. Instead,
the dPMC is sensitive to the metrical structure of a rhythm in so far as it is
implicated in action selection processes depending on the nature of the stimuli. In
study 1, the dPMC is engaged in extracting the relevant auditory features of an
isochronous rhythm in order for subjects to implement or select an appropriately
timed motor response. It is recruited as a function of increasing metric saliency,
that is, it becomes more engaged as auditory features processed in the PT,
progressively convey information of a higher-order nature as was relayed in the
rhythm with the most salient meter. In study 2, neural activity in the dPMC was
greatest for the non-metric rhythm which required greater motor control and
reliance on auditory feedback for the timed selection of movements. In another
words, there was a need for greater interactions between the auditory and motor
systems during performance of the non-metric relative to the other simpler
rhythms. Therefore in studies 1 and 2, dPMC activity is not modulated by the
direct mapping of sounds to movements per se, which remains constant. Instead,

the dPMC is involved in the selection of temporally organized movements based
on higher-order information, such as a rhythm's metric structure, that is extracted
from the auditory cue. In turn, this organization allows for predictability, which is
essential for music perception. Study 3 also revealed that a region at the border of the vPMC and dPMC, the
midPMC, was engaged during the passive perception of musical rhythms, in
addition to listening with anticipation to tap and tap. While in study 3, neural
activity in the midPMC did not correlate with rhythm complexity, findings in
study 2 showed that a region in the vicinity of the midPMC located more
medially, did demonstrate this correlation. In study 2 (Chen et al., 2008) these
findings were reported as being in the vPMC since at that time these results were
labeled according to the well-known division of the premotor cortex into dorsal
and ventral sectors. It was only after the results of study 3 had been examined, we
parceled the premotor cortex into three regions according to their functional
response in that study. Thus the vPMC findings in study 2 (Table 3.1, Chapter 3)
could also be re-categorized as belonging to the midPMC region. However, it is
clearly not anatomically near the vPMC/BA 44 finding reported in study 3 (Table
4.2, Chapter 4) that was interpreted to be involved in direct sensorimotor
transformations. Engagement of the midPMC during passive perception might implicate this
region in the tracking of events over time that may have motor significance. This
interpretation suggests that the midPMC has a higher-order role, similar to that of
the dPMC. Although, neurons in this region have been shown to respond to
auditory stimuli in the anaesthetized monkey (Graziano and Gandhi, 2000), they
are also purported to be engaged in polymodal motion processing (Bremmer et al.,
2001). While more studies need to be conducted to elucidate how the midPMC is
implicated in sensorimotor transformations, a tentative interpretation is that this
border region may perform computations on stimuli that convey composite
information related to both higher-order aspects of sensorimotor processes and its

potential action-related component. Thus, this interpretation may account for the
reported "vPMC" findings in study 2 that were sensitive to rhythm complexity. To summarize, both vPMC and dPMC are important for musical processing, but
they perform distinct and complementary functions as demonstrated by their
functional dissociation in study 3. Listening to music may entail activation of
motor programs associated with producing the music, enabled via vPMC links;
however listening to music also engages a neural system in which dPMC is a
critical node that extracts higher-order metrical information. This latter
mechanism may therefore be critical in setting up temporal (and thus melodic)
expectancies. It is also interesting to note that this premotor dissociation is
reminiscent of, and may be related to behavioural findings showing that disrupted
feedback in terms of period shifts in a sequence result in errors of pitch selection
where as a disruption in timing caused increase timing variability in movement
execution (Pfordresher, 2003a). These behavioural findings were demonstrated
with manipulations of auditory feedback. Therefore, if the vPMC and dPMC are
indeed important for auditory-motor interactions, then it might be predicted that
these regions are even more crucially engaged when feedback is disrupted since
the ability to select movements based on auditory cues is taxed. Prior to concluding this section, it is also worthwhile to note that much evidence
shows the involvement of the vPMC in auditory-motor mappings relevant for
speech. This region has been demonstrated to mediate the mapping of speech
sounds such as phonemes onto its articulatory representations (Devlin and
Watkins, 2007; Meister et al., 2007; Pulvermuller et al., 2006; Wilson and
Iacoboni, 2006; Wilson et al., 2004; Zatorre et al., 1992; Zatorre et al., 1996), and
has also been shown to modulate neural activity in the PT as a function of
increasing syllable articulation rate, even when auditory input was masked (Paus
et al., 1996). Together, these findings support the motor theory of speech
proposed by Liberman et al. (Liberman and Mattingly, 1985). For example,
transcranial magnetic stimulation (TMS) to the left vPMC impairs subjects'

ability to perform a phonetic discrimination task where as in contrast, TMS to the
left STG has no effect (Meister et al., 2007). These findings are also in accordance
with those of patient studies where electrical stimulation ofvPMC interferes with
speech, causing dysarthria (Petrides, 2006), an inability to articulate or coordinate
muscles of the mouth. Similarly, people who stutter, a disorder of motor-speech
fluency also show reduced neural activity in the left vPMC and left Heschl's
gyrus compared to control subjects, and furthermore the integrity of the white
matter underlying the vPMC is also reduced (Watkins et al., 2008). Given that the
vPMC is anatomically connected with auditory regions, these findings suggest
that disruption of these connections may hinder the integration of acoustic inputs
with motor outputs, thereby affecting speech fluency (Watkins et al., 2008). While the evidence reviewed above suggests the importance of the vPMC in
phonological motor processing, the literature is also replete with similar
conclusions regarding posterior parts of Broca's area such as the pars opercularis
(Blumstein et al., 1977; Burton et al., 2000; Demonet et al., 1992; Gough et al.,
2005; Price et al., 1997) that is adjacent to the vPMC. Given that neuroimaging
techniques have limitations in spatial localization, and lesions in patient
populations are often diffuse in nature, careful studies will need to be conducted
to determine the precise contribution of the vPMC and pars opercularis to
phonological processing. In sum, findings from the music, speech and visual domains support the
hypothesis that ventral regions of the premotor system are crucial for directly
matching features of the sensory stimulus with movements, and thus demonstrates
its generic role in these sensorimotor transformations. The dPMC is implicated in
the guidance of movements via auditory (e.g. music) and visual cues bearing
higher-order information; however, studies of speech perception and production
have not generally reported findings in this region. While speech and music share
many similar organizational features, such as their complex and hierarchical

•
•
l
t
structure (J ackendoff and Lerdahl, 2006), it remains to be determined whether the
dPMC has a role in speech processes, and what it would be.
5.3 Other models for auditory-motor transformations
The findings from this dissertation have led to the proposal that the premotor
cortex may be an important node for auditory-motor transformations. However,
there are two other brain regions that have been suggested to mediate these
sensorimotor interactions. One is an auditory region, the PT, which also
encompasses a region located at the border of the Sylvian fissure with the parietal
and temporal lobes (area SPT). The other is the posterior inferior frontal gyrus
involving the mirror neuron system.
5.3.1 Auditory versus Premotor cortex
As described previously, the model of Warren et al. (Warren et al., 2005)
discusses the role of the PT as a computational hub (Griffiths and Warren, 2002)
and extends this further by implicating it in the mediation of generic auditory-
motor transformations. That is, the PT disambiguates spectrotemporal patterns of
the acoustic input, and then relays this information in a sequenced representation
via the dorsal pathway to frontal motor and prefrontal areas where actions are
realized. Therefore this model puts emphasis on the PT for computing these
auditory-motor transformations, and importantly argues that this function is
generic in that any sounds that are of motoric relevance are processed in this
pathway. Another model proposed by Hickok and Poeppel identifies the
importance of area SPT within the PT in mediating sensorimotor integration that
is relevant for the perception and production of speech and music (Hickok and
Poeppel, 2000, 2004, 2007). There are two crucial differences between these
models. First Hickok and Poeppel suggest that the proposed role of the PT as a
computational hub may be separate from the role of area SPT as the interface for
sensorimotor integration (Hickok and Poeppel, 2007). In their studies, area SPT is
defined functionally so that only those voxels that respond to both auditory
stimulation and covert motor rehearsal in the absence of auditory input, are

r
•
t
I
•
~
•
considered to be part of area SPT. Furthermore, they argue that different
subregions demarcated by distinct cytoarchitecture have been found with the
posterior superior temporal plane (Galaburda and Sanides, 1980), and thus it is
possible that they may independently support computational hub and sensorimotor
integration processes. Second, Hickok and Poeppel suggest that area SPT
mediates sensorimotor transformations specifically for the vocal tract motor
system, but that it is also amodal in that non-auditory stimulation, such as that
during silent lip reading, engages this region (Calvert et al., 1997). This idea
stems from their view that area SPT functions more like the parietal system where
different subregions mediate the transformation of sensory information in an
effector-dependent manner (Pa and Hickok, 2007). So for example, the anterior
intraparietal (AlP) area is a sensorimotor integration region for grasping, the
lateral intraparietal (LIP) area is a sensorimotor integration region for eye
movements, and thus area SPT is a sensorimotor integration region for the vocal
tract. I will now address two questions that arise from these models. First, is area SPT
functionally different from the PT? There is general consensus that the dorsal
stream for vision and audition mediates sensorimotor transformations (Hickok and
Poeppel, 2007; Milner and Goodale, 1995; Rizzolatti and Matelli, 2003; Scott and
Johnsrude, 2003; Warren et al., 2005). The question is what is the auditory neural
basis for these transformations? Instead of conceptualizing the PT and area SPT
as mediating two separate functions, computational hub versus sensorimotor
integration, respectively, it may be that the latter could be specialized for the
analysis of spectrotemporal information relevant for sensorimotor
transformations. Therefore, an alternate view is that the sensorimotor systems are
not effector-based, but organized according to the computations carried out by
neurons in the regions of interest (Zatorre and Belin, 2005). A view discussed
extensively in this thesis is that the PT has a generic role in disambiguating
spectrotemporal patterns from an acoustic cue, whether it is spatial, object, or
other in nature (Griffiths and Warren, 2002; Warren et al., 2005; Zatorre and

•
Belin, 2005). Importantly, this more general proposal ofPT function also
encompasses the analysis of spectrotemporal patterns that are relevant for
sensorimotor behaviours (Warren et al., 2005). Thus, findings reported from
studies in the Hickok laboratory (Buchsbaum et al., 2001; Hickok et al., 2003; Okada and Hickok, 2006; Pa and Hickok, 2007) showing recruitment of area SPT
during the perception of speech and melodies, that are subsequently rehearsed,
could be simply interpreted by its involvement in their spectrotemporal analysis. Findings that area SPT also demonstrate motor response properties in that it is
recruited during covert motor rehearsal when auditory input is absent, could be
attributed to motor-to-sensory discharges from the premotor cortex to the PT
(Paus et al., 1996), concurrent auditory imagery during motor rehearsal, or
auditory activation due to the tMRI scanning noise. Importantly, findings reviewed in the introduction and from this thesis
demonstrate engagement of the PT during rhythmic finger tapping tasks that
involve auditory-motor transformations. In particular, evidence from Bengtsson et
al. (Bengtsson et al., 2005) showed that posterior regions of the STG were
engaged in rhythmic production tasks in an effector-independent manner, that is,
whether subjects were tapping out the rhythms or performing rhythmic speech on
one syllable. Thus, these findings would argue against effector specificity of area
SPT for the vocal tract system. Although results from Pa and Hickok suggest a
functional dissociation where area SPT was more engaged during auditory-motor
transformations for vocal than manual effectors, and vice versa for the inferior
parietal sulcus (Pa and Hickok, 2007), the peak voxel of activation in these
regions were quite variable in their anatomical localization across subjects. Therefore, detailed investigations would have to be conducted at an individual
subject level to functionally and anatomically dissociate area SPT within the PT. However, it may also be the case that two functionally separate populations of
neurons could also occupy the same cortical area, making such a task more
challenging. The second question of interest is what is the neural basis for auditory-motor
transformations? Findings from this dissertation show the important role of the
premotor cortex but prior models just discussed implicate auditory regions. Perhaps the process of sensorimotor integration is emergent from computations in
both of these areas. If we assume that area SPT functions like the posterior
parietal cortex as Hickok and Poeppel suggest, then there is plenty of evidence
from the visual domain that demonstrates the role of the parietal cortex in
performing coordinate transformations to map visual representations with motor
output (Buneo and Andersen, 2006). A salient example of this can be found in
patients with optic ataxia whereby lesions in the posterior parietal cortex cause
impairment in reaching under visual guidance despite the absence of any sensory
or motor deficit (Jeannerod et al., 1994). In contrast, patients with visual-form
agnosia caused by lesions in the occipitotemporal cortex are able to accurately
guide hand and finger movements to grasp an object, but demonstrate a profound
inability for object recognition (Goodale et al., 1991). Neurophysiological
evidence in monkeys further show that neurons in both premotor (area F5 in
monkeys) and parietal (AlP) cortex have visual and motor properties (Murata et
al., 1997; Murata et al., 1996), and these regions are extensively connected with
each other (Rizzolatti and Luppino, 2001). Even more interesting is that
functional inactivation of either area F5 or AlP in monkeys produce similar
deficits, namely the inability to shape the hand in an appropriate configuration to
grasp an object (Fogassi et al., 2001; Gallese et al., 1994). The difficulty in
dissociating the contributions of each region has led to the suggestion that the F5-
AIP circuit as a whole plays a crucial role in mediating these visuomotor
transformations, but that area F5 might dominate as it has a more prevalent motor
role than AlP (Raos et al., 2006; Rizzolatti and Luppino, 2001). While Hickok and Poeppel have focused on area SPT, four papers from the
Hickok lab addressing this topic (Buchsbaum et al., 2001; Hickok et al., 2003; Okada and Hickok, 2006; Pa and Hickok, 2007) have also reported the
recruitment of what appears to be ventral premotor regions and Broca's area in

their task that tests for auditory-motor functions of area SPT. Only one of these
papers comment on the findings (Buchsbaum et al., 2001), and none have reported
any functional dissociations: the neural response in premotor cortex is similar to
that of area SPT in that both demonstrate auditory and motor response properties. This is consistent with findings from study 1 where it was demonstrated that
neural activity in the PT and dPMC was temporally correlated and equally
modulated by the stimulus. Findings in studies 2 and 3 also show recruitment of
the PT along with vPMC and dPMC. Taken together, it seems that both auditory
{PT, area SPT) and premotor regions may perform related computations relevant
for sensorimotor transformations, however, it remains an open question regarding
how these areas are functionally dissociated in terms of their precise contribution. Importantly, while Hickok and Poeppel attempt to characterize the parietal/area
SPT system as one of sensorimotor integration that is effector specific, how would
the coordinate transformations mediated in the parietal cortex that have been
extensively studied in the visual domain apply to auditory stimuli, especially if
speech and music do not necessarily involve any spatial processes? Could the PT
perform such 'coordinate transforms' as part of its role in decomposing and
segregating spectrotemporal information from an acoustic stimulus?
5.3.2 Mirror neurons and auditory-motor interactions
Mirror neurons were first studied in the visual domain in monkeys, and are
located in area F5 of the monkey premotor cortex. These neurons become active
when an individual performs an action such as grasping a peanut and when the
individual observes someone else performing the same action (Gallese et al.,
1996; Rizzolatti et al., 1996). In the domain of sound, auditory mirror neurons
also known as echo neurons are engaged when the monkey breaks a peanut and
when he/she hears the sound of a peanut being broken (Keysers et al., 2003; Kohler et al., 2002). Therefore, these neurons are engaged during action and
action-observation. In humans, core regions of the mirror neuron system are
purported to include the vPMC as well as the adjacent pars opercularis (BA 44)
(Rizzolatti and Craighero, 2004), though involvement of the pars triangularis (BA

45) has also been reported. It has been suggested that regions in the posterior
inferior frontal gyrus (IFG), namely BA 44 and BA 45, mediate auditory-motor
mappings during piano playing or singing via the mirror neuron system of action-
recognition (Bangert et al., 2006; Lahav et al., 2007; Mandell et al., 2007). In the
following sections, I will elaborate on the anatomy and function of the mirror
neuron system in relation to that of the vPMC. Mirror and vPMC neurons are
similar, and thus may not be clearly dissociable in the context of findings from
this dissertation that pertain to the auditory domain. Area F5 in the monkey is a premotor area (Craighero et al., 2007). It is critical to
state that three types of neurons have been characterized within area F5: motor
neurons, visuomotor neurons, and mirror neurons (Craighero et al., 2007), and it
is the latter two that concerns this discussion. It was originally reported that the
human homologue of area F5 was in the pars opercularis of Broca's area, that is,
in BA 44 (Rizzolatti and Craighero, 2004). Recent cytoarchitectonic work clearly
shows that BA 44 is not the human homologue of the mirror neuron system
(Craighero et al., 2007; Nelissen et al., 2005; Petrides, 2006; Petrides et al., 2005). In monkeys, mirror neurons are located on the convexity ofthe inferior arcuate
sulcus that has been subcategorized as area F5c; in contrast, visuomotor neurons
are located in the caudal bank of the inferior arcuate sulcus that has been
subcategorized as area F5p (or F5ab) (Figure 5.2, bottom panel). Both of these
regions are agranular and are thus consistent with human cytoarchitecture of the
vPMC. Anterior to the mirror and visuomotor neurons is area F5a that is located
in the fundus of the inferior arcuate sulcus (Figure 5.2, bottom panel). Area F5a
referred to by Nelissen et al. (Nelissen et al., 2005) corresponds to the recently
identified area 44 in the monkey brain by Petrides et al. (Petrides et al., 2005); this
region is dysgranular and is consistent with human cytoarchitecture ofBA 44. Therefore, mirror and visuomotor neurons are located in the posterior border of
the inferior arcuate sulcus in area F5, whose human homologue is the vPMC, and
not Broca's area BA 44 as once thought (Figure 5.2). Findings from study 3 that
were interpreted to be involved in direct auditory-motor transformations also

localized to the border of the vPMC with BA 44, in the inferior precentral sulcus,
that is the human homologue to the inferior arcuate sulcus of the monkey. MONKEY
MONKEY
Figure 5.2
Inferior arcuate sulcus
area F5a
- BA44 human
Convexity: mirror neurons
area F5c - vPMC human
Location of F5 neurons in the monkey. Top two images show the lateral surface
of the monkey and human frontal lobe. Bottom image shows a cross-sectional
view of the inferior arcuate sulcus. In contrast to humans where BA 44 is clearly
seen on the lateral surface of the brain, the monkey homologue area F5a is located
deep in the fundus of the inferior arcuate sulcus. Mirror neurons are a subset ofvisuomotor neurons (Rizzolatti and Luppino,
2001), and it has been suggested that they may have developed from the latter
(Craighero et al., 2007). In fact, mirror and visuomotor neurons are
indistinguishable in terms of their motor properties as both discharge when an
action is executed on an object (Fadiga and Craighero, 2003; Rizzolatti and
Luppino, 2001). However, it is the difference in their sensory properties which
lead to their different functions. Critically, mirror neurons do not discharge at the
mere sight of an object, as importantly, they are only active when there is an

interaction between a biological effector (i.e. hand) and the object (i.e. peanut) of
its manipulation. Thus, a mirror neuron becomes engaged at the observation of an
action performed on an object. On the other hand, visuomotor neurons, also called canonical neurons, are
engaged when an object is manipulated and when the same object is merely
observed (reviewed in Craighero et al., 2007). Importantly these neurons
discharge when the shape and size of the object is congruent with the type of
grasp used to acquire it. These findings have led to the interpretation that there
must be a close correspondence between objects and the actions implemented on
them, that the sight of an object automatically evokes an action to be implemented
on that object, regardless if the action is actually carried out (Craighero et al.,
2007). This has led to the idea that the ''visuomotor coupling shown by canonical
neurons could be at the basis of the sensorimotor transformation that adapts the
hand to a given object" (Fadiga and Craighero, 2003). This interpretation is
consistent with others that have suggested that vPMC neurons match sensory
information with a motor act, and are considered to be involved in direct
sensorimotor mappings (Hoshi and Tanji, 2007; Murata et al., 1997). Thus, it is
precisely the functional properties of these visuomotor neurons from which
evidence has been reviewed in this dissertation that lead to the proposal ofvPMC
involvement in direct visual- and auditory-motor transformations. In particular,
important evidence comes from a study that showed that inactivation of
visuomotor neurons resulted in impaired hand shaping according to the visual
characteristics of the object to be grasped, however, inactivation of mirror neurons
had no effect on hand shaping (Fogassi et al., 2001). These findings clearly
demonstrate that mirror neurons are not engaged in actual sensorimotor
transformations. Instead, "the visuomotor discharge that characterizes mirror
neurons could be at the basis of action imitation and action understanding (Fadiga
and Craighero, 2003)", or in the words ofRizzolatti and Fadiga (Rizzolatti and
Fadiga, 1998) mirror and visuomotor neurons are engaged in "grasping action
meaning versus grasping objects", respectively. In sum, mirror neurons match

self-action with an observed action, while visuomotor neurons match an observed
object with its corresponding action (Fadiga and Craighero, 2003; Hoshi and
Tanji, 2007; Rizzolatti and Luppino, 2001). In the domain sound, auditory mirror neurons are defined as being active
whenever an action-related sound is heard. In this context, it seems that auditory
mirror neurons are relevant during music performance. For example, when a
musician hears the pitch of a specific note, he/she might engage auditory mirror
neurons since this sound has an action associated with it, that is, the finger used to
play the note. This was the interpretation made by some studies that found neural
activity in the posterior IFG and vPMC (Bangert et al., 2006; Lahav et al., 2007). Similarly, the role of auditory mirror neurons in sensorimotor interactions was
contemplated in our review paper, but the focus of that paper was on the premotor
cortex (i.e. visuomotor neurons) and we did not attempt to dissociate these regions
(Zatorre et al., 2007). However, close inspection of the literature in this
dissertation has revealed a conundrum: what population of neurons within the
vPMC might be implicated in sensorimotor integration in the auditory domain? From what was reviewed in the prior paragraphs, it seems clear that visual mirror
neurons are not engaged in the actual 'co-ordinate' transformation or mapping of
sensory information onto motor representations as this is the role of the
visuomotor neurons. Is it valid then, to extrapolate these findings into the auditory
domain, and therefore conclude that auditory mirror neurons do not perform such
transformations but that the posited auditory-motor neurons do? Importantly, it
has not been demonstrated whether there are indeed auditory-motor neurons. Perhaps the association of a sound with an action, proposed to be mediated by
auditory mirror neurons, is synonymous with a transformation that would take
place between the auditory and motor systems to map the sound onto an action. If
this is the case, then there may be no such differentiation between neurons
involved in action-understanding for sounds and auditory-motor transformations
per se. Future studies are required to resolve this issue.

5.4 Role of other motor and prefrontal regions in rhythm perception and
production
The focus of this dissertation has been on auditory-motor coupling. This is one
component operation among several others such as working memory, sequencing
and timing that are all likely engaged during the perception and production of
musical rhythm. This next section will aim to dissect the neural basis for these
processes and show that they rely on brain regions other than the premotor cortex,
which this thesis demonstrates is specifically recruited for auditory-motor
interactions.
5.4.1 Neural basis for the serial and hierarchical organization of musical
rhythm
A rhythm unravels sequentially, such that each event is related to another in a
serial and/or hierarchical manner. Therefore by definition, rhythmic events are
necessarily parsed in a sequential fashion, and working memory mechanisms may
also be engaged to help monitor and track each acoustic input in relation to
another. These processes may in part rely upon the prefrontal cortex that is
suggested to be hierarchically organized along a rostral-caudal axis (Badre and
D'Esposito, 2007; Fiebach and Schubotz, 2006; Koechlin and Jubault, 2006; Petrides, 2005b) such that information computed by these regions then feed into
the frontal motor areas where actions such as rhythmic tapping can be
implemented (Koechlin and Jubault, 2006; Petrides, 2005b ). This proposal is also
supported by anatomical work showing a cascade-like network of anatomical
connectivity from prefrontal to motor cortex (reviewed in Picard and Strick,
2001). For example, projections from the prefrontal cortex travel to rostral sectors
of the dPMC and pre-SMA, which then project to their respective caudal
counterparts from where inputs finally arrive at primary motor cortex. One relevant model has focused on the rostral-caudal gradient of executive
control involving BA 45, BA 44 and the vPMC (Koechlin and Jubault, 2006; Koechlin et al., 2003). According to this model, these regions are involved in

action selection or inhibition based on a nested hierarchy of action plans. Concurrent with the prior discussion in this thesis, the vPMC is engaged in a
single sensorimotor act, that is, the selection of an action based on a sensory cue. In contrast, the adjacent BA 44 selects or inhibits simple action chunks that
comprise individual sensorimotor acts. Finally, BA 45 exerts superordinate
control on the action chunks. Therefore, these regions may be involved in the
hierarchical organization of rhythm by mediating the parsing of chunks within a
sequence and the higher-level understanding of meter. This model can account for
findings in the neuroimaging literature that have found recruitment of regions
along the inferior frontal gyrus during tasks of rhythm and/or sequence perception
and production (for example, Bangert et al., 2006; Lahav et al., 2007). Furthermore, it is also in accord with proposal ofBA 44 and vPMC as a sequence
processor (Fiebach and Schubotz, 2006). However, one important limitation of
this model is that the prefrontal cortex is not posited to control the timings
involved in action selection and sequencing. In fact, the lack of inferior frontal
recruitment in studies 2 and 3 confirm that these regions were insensitive to the
temporal organization of a rhythm. But, this is an integral factor that must be
accounted for in any model of rhythm processing. Instead, it has been proposed that the pre-SMA and SMA play an important role
in the temporal control of moven1ent sequences. Interestingly these regions are
also hypothesized to construct individual motor acts such as reaching, grasping
and holding, into a unified and sequenced motor action like eating (Rizzolatti et
al., 2001). Electrophysiological findings show that 1) both pre-SMA and SMA
neurons show selective activity for specific sequences of actions, 2) SMA neurons
are selectively active for the intervals between actions in a sequence, and 3) pre-
SMA neurons show selective activity for the order of actions in a sequence
(Shima and Tanji, 2000; Tanji and Shima, 1994). These results suggest that these
medial premotor neurons carry information about the temporal order and
consequent planning of upcoming multiple movements. Damage to, or ablation of,
the SMA and pre-SMA in humans (Halsband et al., 1993) and monkeys (Chen et

al., 1995; Shima and Tanji, 1998) also impairs the ability to execute sequences of
movements from memory but does not impair non-sequential reaching
movements. Using TMS in humans, Kennerely et al. (Kennerley et al., 2004) also elegantly
demonstrated that the pre-SMA is not involved in the execution of every
movement in a sequence, but rather its role involves the organization of action
sequences into smaller chunks, or superordinate sets. TMS disrupted performance
only when applied at the beginning of a sequence or at a chunk point, but no
impairment resulted when TMS was applied at a non-chunk point in a sequence. As suggested by Kennerley et al., the role of the pre-SMA in sequence chunking
is likely part of a more general function of this region in updating action plans;
whether it be switching between different motor sequences, or chunks within a
sequence, this region is most active when there is a need to change from a current
to a new motor plan (Matsuzaka and Tanji, 1996; Rushworth et al., 2002; Shima
et al., 1996). On the other hand, neurons in the SMA do not show preferential
activity for the updating of action plans, but are selectively active for effector
control during actual motor execution (Hoshi and Tanji, 2004; Matsuzaka et al.,
1992; Matsuzaka and Tanji, 1996; Shima et al., 1996; Shima and Tanji, 2000). TMS to the SMA has also been shown to impair performance on complex
sequences but not repetitive or consecutive (i.e. scale) finger tapping (Gerloff et
al., 1997), suggesting that the former may tax temporal control of movements
more than the latter. Findings from study 2, replicated in study 3, showed that as the complexity of a
rhythm's temporal structure increases, neural activity in the preSMA and SMA
also increases. These results were interpreted to relate to the role of these medial
motor regions in temporal sequencing. Each rhythm in this study contained eleven
elements that could be grouped, or chunked into smaller units. The metric simple
rhythm was constructed so that chunking would be easily facilitated since all
elements were arranged to fall in time with a predicted temporal grid according to

the model by Povel and Essens (Povel and Essens, 1985). On the other extreme,
chunking in the non-metric rhythm was posited be the most difficult since this
rhythm was the least temporally structured, and would consequently hinder
subjects from finding an obvious grouping strategy. Therefore results from study
2 may be interpreted by reference to the fact that the need to chunk events in time
is progressively taxed as a function of metrical complexity which in turn affects
motor execution. Furthermore, results from study 3 showed that the SMA was
even sensitive to the passive perception of these same sequences, regardless of
complexity. Thus, it appears that this region might inherently be interested in
tracking events across time. In contrast, neither the preSMA nor SMA was
recruited in study 1 where temporal sequencing was not manipulated. While parts of the frontal lobe along the inferior frontal gyrus have been
implicated in the processing of nested action hierarchies as discussed earlier, other
regions may have a role in rhythmic sequencing, specifically in those with
musical expertise. An interesting result from study 2 was that neural activity in
the preSMA and SMA was no different between subjects with musical training
versus those without. On the one hand this result might be surprising since
musicians are experts at the execution of rhythmic sequences with high temporal
precision, suggesting that this ability may be correlated with neural activity in the
preSMA/SMA. However, it was found that musicians compared to non-musicians
recruited greater neural activity in the mid-dorsolateral prefrontal cortex (DLPFC)
and a region at the border of the mid-ventrolateral prefrontal cortex (VLPFC) with
BA 44. While recruitment ofBA 44 could also be related to the selection of action
chunks discussed earlier, another hypothesis is that working memory mechanisms
that engage the DLPFC and VLPFC may support the superior performance of
musicians in rhythmic sequencing. Knowledge of how musical time is organized
(i.e. in a metrical framework) allows musicians to retrieve and monitor individual
events that comprise a rhythm. These processes are mediated by the VLPFC and
DLPFC respectively (Petrides, 2005a) with perhaps concurrent processing of this
information by the preSMA/SMA in translating it into a sequenced movement

representation. Then, according to a model of frontal lobe cognitive control by
Petrides (Petrides, 2005b ), this higher-order information is directed to the
premotor and motor cortex where the selection and consequent implementation of
a precisely timed motor response occurs. Indeed, evidence has suggested that
rostral sectors of the dPMC are implicated in the conversion of working memory
information into a sequenced motor plan (Ohbayashi et al., 2003). Non-musicians
on the other hand, rely on basic strategies such as chunking (Sakai et al., 2004),
for example by temporal proximity (Bregman, 1999). This approach is thought to
be mediated by the preSMA (Kennerley et al., 2004), and may be less dependent
on functions of the prefrontal cortex since for non-musicians, there is no precise
representation of each rhythmic event to be retrieved and monitored. Support for
this idea comes from a study that showed improved working memory
performance related to increased neural activity in DLPFC in only those subjects
trained to be experts in identifying abstract visual objects that were subsequently
tested in a working memory task (Moore et al., 2006). Similarly, other evidence
suggests that a trained expert ability for chunking in non-musical contexts also
engages the DLPFC (Bor et al., 2003; Bor and Owen, 2006, 2007). Therefore it
remains to be determined whether development of expertise at musical sequence
chunking in non-musicians would also implicate active retrieval and monitoring
mechanisms of the prefrontal cortex. In sum, it seems that both BA 44/BA 45 and the preSMA/SMA share similar roles
in the serial and hierarchical processing of sequences such as rhythms. While the
inferior frontal regions seem to be sensitive to the nested hierarchy of action
plans, it is the preSMA/SMA region that is not only sensitive to how events
within a sequence are grouped, but crucially, to their temporal ordering. Perhaps
the time-independent organization of action sequences mediated by the former is
relayed to the latter where actions are then coded in a temporally precise manner. This functional hypothesis is supported by known anatomical connections linking
these two regions (reviewed in Petrides, 2006). Lastly, working memory function
of the DLPFC and VLPFC may also be relevant in exerting executive control over

the precise ordering of each rhythmic event in those with explicit knowledge of
how to retrieve and monitor such events.
5.4.2 Neural basis for the timing of musical rhythm
Much research has been devoted to the investigation of how timing mechanisms
are instantiated in the brain. It has been hypothesized that there might be a
centralized timer, however several brain regions have now been implicated in
temporal processing including the prefrontal cortex, the SMA, parietal cortex,
cerebellum and basal ganglia (Buhusi and Meek, 2005; lvry and Spencer, 2004; Lewis and Miall, 2003; Mauk and Buonomano, 2004). Such a distributed cortical
and subcortical representation could be related to the idea that different operations
might be engaged depending on the time span (e.g. millisecond or hours) studied. More specifically, it has been proposed that time might be an emergent property
of the nature and modality of the task, which suggests that any neural region
might be capable of computing temporal information (lvry and Spencer, 2004; Mauk and Buonomano, 2004). What is relevant for this discussion concerns
timing at the millisecond level where events comprising a rhythmic sequence
occur. The discussion will focus on the cerebellum and basal ganglia, two regions
whose involvement in the timing of rhythmic sequences has been much debated. Previous neuroimaging and patient studies have implicated the cerebellum in
auditory-motor rhythm synchronization (Jancke et al., 2000a; Mayville et al.,
2002; Rao et al., 1997; Thaut, 2003), rhythm reproduction (Bengtsson et al., 2005;
lvry and Keele, 1989; lvry et al., 1988; Penhune et al., 1998; Spencer et al., 2003; Theoret et al., 2001), rhythm perception (Sakai et al., 1999), and the reproduction
and/or perception of discrete temporal intervals (lvry and Keele, 1989; Jueptner et
al., 1995; Lewis and Miall, 2003; Mangels et al., 1998). Two studies specifically
examined the effect of cerebellar lesions on auditory-motor rhythmic tapping and
showed that the ability to synchronize motor responses with an isochronous
auditory cue was not dependent on the cerebellum (Diedrichsen et al., 2003; Molinari et al., 2005). That is, patients with cerebellar lesions were able to

maintain an inter-tap-interval that did not significantly deviate from the
interstimulus interval, and were also able to synchronize with tone onsets. However, tapping variability was increased in patients compared to healthy
control subjects. These findings are consistent with those found using
reproduction tasks where patients tap without sensory cueing (Ivry and Keele,
1989; Ivry et al., 1988). Although, a provocative study conducted by Harrington
et al. (Harrington et al., 2004) also showed the same finding but attributed the
increased variability to a correlated measure of working memory, thus questioning
the role of the cerebellum in timekeeping mechanisms. Nonetheless, motor
entrainment to auditory cues appears to be unaffected by lesions in the cerebellum
which have led some to suggest that the actual on-line control of timing may be
mediated by other neural regions (unspecified by the authors) (Diedrichsen et al.,
2003; Molinari et al., 2005). For example, this thesis argues that the dPMC may
have a role in the selection of temporally organized movements based on sensory
cues, and the preSMNSMA is involved in the temporal sequencing of
movements. Rather than conceptualize the role of the cerebellum in temporal processing, a
more parsimonious view has been to attribute its function to feedforward control
(Fuentes and Bastian, 2007; Ohyama et al., 2003; Wolpert et al., 1998) that would
encompass computations involving time (Mauk and Buonomano, 2004; Ohyama
et al., 2003). In this model, the cerebellum is a sensorimotor predictor that
interprets sensory information (Bower, 1995; Nixon, 2003; Petacchi et al., 2005)
and receives efference copies of motor commands, in order to optimize motor
outputs. Therefore, the role of the cerebellum in sensorimotor interactions differs
from that of the premotor cortex, as it is interested in the fine-tuning of
movements to allow one to precisely time a motor response and thus improve
movement accuracy. Support for this idea also comes from studies that have
shown engagement of the cerebellum during the early stages of motor sequence
learning, but not when sequences have been well-learned and thus motor

optimization is no longer as crucial (Penhune and Doyon, 2002, 2005; Penhune et
al., 1998). Modulation of cerebellar neural activity in relation to temporal processing was
demonstrated in study 2. Neural activity in left cerebellar lobule VI increased as
tapping performance became poorer as a function of increasing rhythm
complexity, in both non-musicians and musicians. Musicians additionally showed
recruitment of left lobule VIlla. Progressive engagement of the cerebellum was
interpreted to reflect the increasing need for sensorimotor integration (i.e. the use
of auditory information to predict and optimize when each tap should precisely
occur). An interesting finding from study 3 showed that bilateral lobule VI was
also recruited during the passive perception of musical rhythms. This finding is in
line with the notion that the cerebellum is engaged in the sensory acquisition of
data (Bower, 1995; Petacchi et al., 2005), comparing it with the actual sensory
outcome that in tum may be relevant for motor behaviour (Blakemore et al., 2001; Blakemore et al., 1998; Nixon, 2003). In addition, bilateral lobules VI and VIlla
were also engaged during listen with anticipation and tap. In sum findings from
studies 2 and 3 suggest that lobules VI and VIlla may be implicated in
sensorimotor integration relevant for temporal processing. Indeed this
interpretation concurs with results of a meta-analysis that also proposes
involvement of lobules VI and VIlla in timing and sensory processes
(Schmahmann, 2000). In contrast, these regions were not engaged in study 1. In
this study subjects synchronized their tapping with isochronous rhythms where
according to the above interpretation, sensorimotor integration for temporal
processing would not be taxed. Instead, comparison of simple isochronous finger
tapping relative to a silence baseline engaged only right lobule V, which is
suggested to mediate processes related to basic motor control of the ipsilateral
effector (Schmahmann, 2000). A number of neuroimaging studies have also found recruitment of the basal
ganglia in tasks involving timing (Grahn and Brett, 2007; Lewis et al., 2004; Rao

et al., 1997; Rao et al., 2001). In particular, it has been proposed that the basal
ganglia might mediate the actual encoding of temporal intervals (Rao et al., 2001). This idea would be consistent with studies that have manipulated dopamine levels
in rats and humans, and shown it to affect the perception of a time interval at the
seconds level, by slowing it down or speeding it up (Buhusi and Meek, 2005). On
the other hand, evidence from patient studies show inconsistent findings. Some
have found people with Parkinson's disease to be impaired on various timing
tasks and manifest deficits similar to those seen in patients with cerebellar lesions
(Artieda et al., 1992; Harrington et al., 1998; O'Boyle et al., 1996; Pastor et al.,
1992a; Pastor et al., 1992b ). Others have shown that these patients perform the
same as healthy control subjects (Aparicio et al., 2005; Ivry and Keele, 1989; Spencer and Ivry, 2005). In fact, it has been argued that the study of basal ganglia
function via Parkinson's disease may lead to findings that could be attributed to
impaired function in other cortical regions that are also affected by the
dopaminergic system (Aparicio et al., 2005). More recently it has been suggested
that the basal ganglia may have a role in the regulation of force that may be
related to accurate timing, but has no specific role in timing per se (Aparicio et al.,
2005; Pope et al., 2005; Pope et al., 2006). Similar to the cerebellum, current theories about basal ganglia function suggest
that it is unlikely that it is exclusively engaged in temporal processing (Buhusi
and Meek, 2005; Ivry and Spencer, 2004). While the cerebellum is proposed to
mediate supervised learning in a feedforward manner through trial and error, the
basal ganglia are posited to have a role in reinforcement learning that promotes
optimal motor control via reward signals encoded by dopamine (Doya, 2000; Graybiel, 2005). The striatal beat-frequency model of interval timing by Meek
proposes that the basal ganglia act as a coincidence detector such that its
activation results from simultaneous cortical neural inputs (Buhusi and Meek,
2005). More specifically, oscillatory cortical activity is synchronized at trial onset,
possibly signalled by dopaminergic activity in the substantia nigra pars compacta,
and continues to oscillate throughout the interval until the time of reward and/or

feedback. Therefore temporal coding is an emergent property of coincident
detection. Ivry and Spencer have also put forth a similar idea that proposes the
basal ganglia are involved in a threshold-gated decision making process
modulated by dopamine (lvry and Spencer, 2004). The basal ganglia receive
cortical inputs that are gated, and only when a criterion level of activation is
reached, selected representations such as the updating of working memory are
then implemented. In this model, the judgment of time is achieved by determining
the number of updates in working memory in terms of pulses of time emitted by a
pacemaker (Gibbon et al., 1984). In sum, both of these models provide a neural basis for cognitive (Lewis and
Miall, 2003) or interval timing (Buhusi and Meek, 2005), that is, when a discrete
interval is perceived, judged and/or reproduced, and these mechanisms may be
reward-based as they are modulated by the dopamine system. On the other hand,
several studies on rhythm processing have not pointed to a specific role for the
basal ganglia in the encoding of multiple and sequential intervals that define a
rhythmic sequence (Bengtsson et al., 2004, 2005; Jancke et al., 2000a; Penhune et
al., 1998; Ramnani and Passingham, 2001; Sakai et al., 1999; Sakai et al., 2004). Similarly, studies in this thesis show that the basal ganglia are recruited during
rhythmic tapping, but in a non-temporally specific manner. It is engaged in a
general sense when tapping is compared to a baseline rest condition, but its neural
activity is not modulated by any temporal manipulation. Furthermore, it has also
been posited that the basal ganglia are engaged when rhythms or motor sequences
are highly learned such that they form a motor program (Doyon and Benali, 2005; Penhune and Doyon, 2002). This suggests its important role in motor control and
not timing per se. S. S Conclusions
S. S.l Summary
In order to study the influence of sounds on motor behaviour, the experiments in
this dissertation capitalize on the fact that rhythm and movements are closely

linked. Prior models of auditory-motor interactions were essentially based on
evidence from the speech and auditory spatial domains of study, and only focused
on the contributions of auditory regions. This thesis provides evidence that other
classes of complex sounds such as a musical rhythm engage the dorsal pathway
for auditory-guided actions. More importantly, this thesis proposes that different
regions of the premotor cortex interface with auditory cues to result in the
selection of temporally organized movements especially relevant for music
perception and production.
5.5.2 Future Directions
Questions for further research have been raised throughout the discussion, but a
few of the essential ones include the following:
1) How does the transformation of auditory information into a motor
representation occur, what is the mechanism? If transformations are an emergent
property of sensory and motor computations carried out in the planum temporale
and premotor cortex respectively, what are the precise contributions of each
region?
2) Do auditory-motor feedback processes also engage the same neural circuitry
and how are these mediated?
3) Is the premotor cortex truly generic in its ability to interface sensory cues: does
it perform computations regardless of stimulus domain (music, speech) and
modality (auditory, visual)? Evidence reviewed in this dissertation suggests that it
is. So what are the computational properties of this vast region of cortex? Are
there differences in cytoarchitecture within this agranular area that might lead to
further functional subdivisions?
4) How might auditory-premotor interactions and the anatomical connectivity
between these regions differ between humans and non-human primates? Could
differences, if any, be related to why humans went on to develop the unique
ability for language and music?

5) What is the neural basis for auditory-motor entrainment in terms of its use as a
rehabilitation tool? Is it simply the case that stimulation of the auditory and
premotor systems can facilitate the amelioration and recovery of movements? In conclusion, findings from this dissertation are promising in that they have shed
some light on how auditory cues can drive motor actions, an operation relevant
for speech, music and rehabilitation. However, much work remains to be done so
that we can understand complex human behaviours such as how we tap to the
beat. Furthermore, continued research in this field might lead to improved and
new rehabilitation techniques in the treatment of movement dysfunction. References

REVIEWS
When the brain plays music:
auditory-motor interactions in
music perception and production
Robert J Zatorre **, Joyce L. Chen ** and Virginia B. Penhune§*
Abstract I Music performance is both a natural human activity, present in all societies, and
one of the most complex and demanding cognitive challenges that the human mind can
undertake. Unlike most other sensory-motor activities, music performance requires precise
timing of several hierarchically organized actions, as well as precise control over pitch
interval production, implemented through diverse effectors according to the instrument
involved. We review the cognitive neuroscience literature of both motor and auditory
domains, highlighting the value of studying interactions between these systems in a musical
context. and propose some ideas concerning the role of the premotor cortex in integration
of higher order features of music with appropriately timed and organized actions. Whether it is a child singing 'Happy Birthday; or a con-
cert pianist interpreting a Brahms concerto, the neural
mechanisms involved in producing and perceiving music
provide a rich source of questions for cognitive neuro-
science. The interaction between auditory and motor
systems is of particular interest, because each action in
a performance produces sound, which influences each
subsequent action, leading to remarkable sensory-motor
interplay (FIG. 1 ). Although much research has been car-
ried out into sensory- motor interactions in processes
such as reaching and grasping and speech, these actions
do not fu lly capture the requirements of musical execu-
tion. Performing even a simple musical piece requires
precise control of timing over an extended period in
order to follow a hierarchical rhythmic structure, and also
requires the musician to control pitch so as to produce
specitk musical intervals (frequency ratios), which is not
relevant in speech (even tonal languages do not rely on
specific intervals, but rather on pitch contours). Thus,
music makes some unique demands on the nervous
system, an understanding of which should in turn help
to reveal particular aspects of neuronal function. In this
Review, we provide an overview of what is known so far
about motor control and tonal perception as applied to
music, followed by a discussion of the neural mecha-
nisms that may mediate their interaction. We conclude
with some hypotheses about the functional architecture
involved in music perception and production, and
suggest some ideas for iuture work. Music production: motor control systems
When a musician performs, at least three basic motor
control functions are required: timing, sequencing and
spatial organization of movement. The accurate timing
of movements is related to the organization of musi-
cal rhythm, whereas sequencing and spatial aspects of
movement relate to playing individual notes on a musi-
cal instrument. Although a large number of studies have
examined the neural systems underlying these functions
separately, Little is known about how they work together
to produce a complex musical performance. In addition,
there is considerable debate regarding both the defini-
tion of these motor parameters and the specific contri-
butions of particular brain regions to their control. The
study of music production requires these systems to be
studied in an integrated fashion, thus making it both a
challenging and fruitful model system for research into
sensory- motor integration. Timing. The neural mechanisms that underlie the timing
of movement have been intensively studied over the past
20 years, but currently there is more controversy than
consensus in this field. The ability to time movement
precisely has been attributed to a neural clock or counter
mechanism in which time is represented through pulses
or oscillations'-', but it has also been hypothesized to
be an emergent property of the kinematics oi move-
ment itselP·;·6• Functional neuroimaging studies, as
well as studies of brain -damaged patients, have linked

figure illustrates the feedback and feedforward interactions that occur during music
performance. As a musician plays an instrument. motor systems control the fine
movements needed to produce sound. The sound is processed by auditory circuitry,
which in turn is used to adjust motor output to achieve the desired effect. Output signals
from premotor cortices are also thought to influence responses within the auditory
cortex. even in the absence of sound, or prior to sound: conversely. motor representations
are thought to be active even in the absence of movement on hearing sound. There is
therefore a tight linkage between sensory and production mechanisms. Pitch

movement timing to several cortical and sub-cortical
regions, including the cerebellum, basal ganglia and
supplementary motor area (SMA). It has been proposed
that the basal ganglia and possibly the SMA may be
more important for interval timing at longer timescales
(I second and above), whereas th e cerebellum may be
more important tor controlling motor timing at shorter
timescales (millisecond)L7•
Studies have shown that patients with cerebellar
lesions have an impaired ability to complete perceptual
and motor timing tasks", and neuroimaging studies
have shown cerebellar activity in relation to movement
timing"·'0 Although some studies have failed to sup-
port a direct contribution of the cerebellum to timing",
current theories of cerebellar function suggest it may
have a role in feedforward control or error correction
-
both of these functions would be relevant for timing. Several researchers have proposed that the cerebellum
computes predictive models of movement that would
include movement timing" ·!.', whereas others suggest
that it is most important for online error correction
© 2007 Nature Publishing Group
based on feedback, which would also contribute to opti-
mization of timing"- The cerebellum may contribute to
the precise control of movement trajectories, which are
related to accurate timing,15·16, and it has been shown to
have a role in the acquisition and integration of sensory
information". When subjects perform purely auditory
perceptual tasks, neuroimaging studies consistently
show cerebellar activity!". Studies have suggested that the basal ganglia arc also
directly involved in movement timing. Patients with
Parkinson's disease, who have damage in the basal ganglia
system, show impaired movement timing19• Furthermore,
neuroirnaging studies have shown that the basal ganglia
are active in tasks that require timed finger tapping20•21•
It has also been suggested that the basal ganglia may be
involved in controlling specific motor parameters, such
as force, which contribute to accurate timing22. Many of these studies have examined very simple
rhythms, usually requiring participants to tap a single
finger to a constant beat. Although such tasks reveal
important basic properties of perceptual and motor
timing, it is not clear whether neural models based on
these simple tasks are adequate for complex tasks like
musical performance. Several recent experiments have
examined perception and reproduction of more com-
plex musical rhythms. These studies have shown greater
involvement of the dorsal premotor cortex (dPMC),
lateral cerebellar hemispheres and the prefrontal cor-
tex23·2425. It is not known whether these changes in brain
activity are directly related to the temporal complexity
of the rhythms or to other parameters such as sequence
complexity, or the degree to which rhythmic structure
allows subjects to predict and organize their motor per-
formance. These results indicate that motor timing is
not controlled by a single brain region, but by a network
of regions that control specific parameters of movement
and that depend on the relevant timescale of the rhyth-
mic sequence. High-level control of sequence execution
appears to involve the basal ganglia, PMC and SMA,
whereas fine-grain correction of individual movements
may be controlled by the cerebellum. Sequencing. Motor sequencing has been explored in
terms of either the ordering of individual movements,
such as finger sequences for key presses, or the coor-
dination of subcomponents of complex multi-joint
movements. Several cortical and sub-cortical regions,
including the basal ganglia, the SMA and the pre-
SMA, the cerebellum, and the premotor and prefrontal
cortices, have been implicated in the production and
learning of motor sequences, but their specific contribu-
tions and the way they work together are not yet clear. Neurophysiological studies in animals have demonstrated
an interaction between the frontal cortex and basal
ganglia during the learning of movement sequences26
Human neuroimaging studies have also emphasized
the contribution of the basal ganglia for well-learned
sequences2'. It has been argued that the cerebellum is
important for sequence learning and for the integration
of individual movements into unified sequences27•2s- 3l,
whereas the pre-SMA and SMA have been shown to

be involved in organizing or chunking of more complex
movement sequences3v 3. Finally, the premotor cortex
has been shown to be involved in tasks that require the
production of relatively complex sequences, and it may
contribute to motor prediction 34·35 • Sequencing has also
been studied in a more musical context in an experiment
that examined neural activity during the execution of
sequences of key-presses that differed either in temporal
or sequential complexity13• This study showed that more
complex sequences required activity from the basal
ganglia, dPMC and cerebellum. Spatial organization. Expert musical performance
requires precise spatial organization of movements. Few studies of complex motor control have distin -
guished between the spatial and sequential compo-
nents of a series of movements. Studies in animals and
humans have established the involvement of parietal,
sensory-motor and premotor cortices in the control of
movements when the integration of spatial, sensory and
motor information is required36·37• More recent work has
suggested that separate neural systems may underlie the
ability to learn and produce the spatial and sequential
components of a complex task29·3"- Surprisingly, few stud-
ies have explicitly examined tl1e role of spatial processing
in the context of musical tasks. A behavioural study of
spatial accuracy in trained cellists found that they do
not show the typical distance/accuracy trade-off for
finger movements while playing39• A recent neuroimag-
ing study contrasting sequential and temporal sequence
learning23 suggested that the dPMC may have a role
in the learning of spatial trajectories. Overall, however,
the contribution of spatial processing to music-related
motor tasks remains an area in which future work could
make an important contribution. Music perception: auditory processing streams
Considerable progress has been made in models of
auditory cortex anatomy. The scenario now emerging
is that of a hierarchical system in which several distinct
pathways emerge from the primary auditory cortex
(A I), projecting towards different targets40•41 • There is at
least one stream projecting ventrally from A I within tl1e
temporal neocortex, and quite possibly a second stream
projecting anteriorly along the superior temporal gyrus
(STG)'c. Another stream follows a more dorsal and pos-
terior course, reaching parietal targets. The functional
properties of these pathways are less clear. One model
suggests that ventral and dorsal streams may parallel the
visual system in supporting object and spatial processing,
respectively<'. As discussed below, the dorsal stream may
also be conceptualized as playing a part in auditory-motor
transformations43, analogous to the role proposed for the
visual dorsal stream44 • A related view is that dorsal areas
may track changes in spectral energy over time, offering
a functional parallel to vision, insofar as retinotopic and
cocl11eotopic mapping may require similar cortical com-
putational mechanisms". According to these views, the
dorsal auditory cortical pathway is relevant for spatial
processing, and tracks time-varying events. Therefore, a
link to motor systems would make sense, as movements

## NATURE REVI EWS I NEUR O SCIENCE

© 2007 Nature Publishing Group
REVIEWS
occur in time as well as in space. Conversely, ventral path-
ways are thought to be specialized for invariant auditory
object properties46·", which are time-independent48, and
therefore less related to motor systems. Pitch. One of the most salient features of sound relevant
for music is pitch. Neurons lateral to A 1 in the marmoset
were tound to be sensitive specifically to the fundamental
frequency of a complex tone49, suggesting that pitch
constancy may be enabled by such a neural mechanism. The importance of cortical regions lateral to A 1 for pitch
coding is also supported by human lesion and functional
magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) studies50-52• These
data suggest a hierarchical system for pitch processing,
with more abstract properties of the stimulus encoded as
one proceeds along the processing streams. The precise
nature of this coding becomes less well understood for
more distal components of the streams, but patterns of
pitches unfolding over time -
that is, a melody -
are
known to engage neural populations in both anterior
and posterior auditory pathways53. Such results sug-
gest that different parameters of a tune (such as global
contour, specific interval sizes or local duration ratios
of tones) might be processed in the different streams. It is uncertain what specific computations the posterior
regions are carrying out, but we postulate that sensitivity
to temporal expectations might be one such function, in
accordance v.-ith the idea that posterior auditory regions
have a privileged link to motor regions. Hem ispheric asymmetries. Lateralization of cortical
responses is also an important aspect of tonal processing,
v.-ith much empirical data favouring a right-hemisphere
advantage tor tonal functions. One explanation for this
phenomenon is that hemispheric asymmetries arise
from fundamental differences in acoustical processing
(such as spectrotemporal resolution54 or time integration
windows55) -
neuroimaging studies in which acoustical
features are manipulated tend to support such a view'5•56•
Alternatively, hemispheric differences may be related
to abstract knowledge domains, such as languages7. These views are not mutually exclusive58 but, regardless
of the model, the stage within the processing streams
at which such hemispheric differences are manifested
remains poorly understood, leading one to ask how
tl1ese processing differences influence neural operations
in downstream areas. Therefore, one key question is the
extent to which lateralization of perceptual processes may
influence lateralization of motor processes, as these have
mostly been studied independently so far59• Moreover,
top-down influences of abstract knowledge, such as
musical syntax, may also have important implications60
for patterns oflaterality. Rhythm. In addition to pitch or melody, music relies
on rhythm. Behavioural studies demonstrate that
rhythm and pitch can be perceived separately6 ', but
that they also interact•~ in creating a musical percept. Neuropsychological studies indicate that these dimen-
sions may be separable in the brain: patients with brain
injury may be impaired in the processing of melody but

Figure 2 I Hierarchical metrical structure in a familiar song. Regular metrical
structure is a common feature of music from many cultures. It consists of a hierarchical
framework of perceived beats that is inferred from the acoustic stimulus. and unfolds
over equal units of time. This structure is illustrated in the song 'Norwegian Wood'. Each column of Xs represents a beat; each row of Xs corresponds to different
hierarchical levels of temporal regularity, from the lowest level. which relates to local
regularities. to higher levels. which occur in integer multiples of the lower levels. and
correspond to more global temporal regularities. When listening to a piece of music,
most people. regardless of formal musical training. can extract this periodic higher
order organization of events that allows one to create temporal expectancies. and thus
tap to the beat of the tune. Several theoretical models exist of how this metrical
structure is extracted from ongoing sound". Figure modified. with permission. from
